Prohibition of Disposing of Salam Capital or Commodity Before Receipt
Lists acts prohibited regarding salam capital and commodity before delivery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of disposing of salam capital or commodity before receipt, list the specific forms of prohibited disposal, and explain the legal rationale based on hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Disposal of salam capital or commodity before physical receipt is haram, based on the principle: do not sell what you do not possess.

This lesson explores the prohibition of disposing of the capital (ra's al-mal) or the salam commodity (muslam fih) before actual receipt (qabd). The commentator enumerates specific acts—sale, gift, partnership, assignment, substitution—and explains the underlying reasoning: the salam commodity is analogous to a sold item, and the capital is due for receipt in the session. Understanding this rule ensures the validity of the salam contract and protects against transactions invalidated by premature disposal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the statement: 'It is not valid to dispose of the capital or the salam commodity before receipt.' This is the core prohibition.
2
List the forms of prohibited disposal
For capital: taking something else in exchange, gifting. For salam commodity: partnership, sale, gift, assignment (tawliyah), sale at profit (murabahah), substitution.
3
Understand the rationale
The salam commodity is treated as a sold item, and the hadith prohibits selling movable goods before taking possession. The capital must be received in the session, so disposal forfeits that right.
4
Note the boundary conditions
'Before receipt' means before the seller receives the capital and before the buyer receives the commodity. The prohibition applies even if the disposal is to the original seller.
5
Apply to the example
A man advances 10 dirhams for a kurr of wheat. Another offers 5 dirhams to partner before receipt. The partnership is invalid because it is a disposal of the salam commodity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصح التصرّف في رأس المال والمسلم فيه قبل القبض بشركة أي على مسلم إليه أو تولية. أو مرابحة ولو ممن عليه قبل الافتراق بالأبدان أي افتراق كل واحد من المتعاقدين عندنا. (عيني، فتح) ولا يصح التصرف إلخ ومن صور التصرف في رأس المال : أن يأخذ بدل رأس المال شيئاً آخر، ومنها أيضاً: أن يهب رأس المال من رب السلم قبل القبض. ومن صور التصرف في المسلم فيه قبل القبض: أن يشرك غيره فيه أو يبيعه أو يهبه قبل القبض، وجه عدم جواز التصرف أن المسلم فيه مبيع، والتصرف في المبيع المنقول قبل القبض لا يجوز، ورأس المال مستحق القبض في المجلس، والتصرف فيه مفوت له فلا يجوز والتصرف المنفي يشمل البيع، حتى لو باع رب السلم المسلم فيه من المسلم إليه بأكثر من رأس المال لا يصح، ويشمل الاستبدال حتى إذا استبدل المسلم فيه بجنس آخر لا يجوز؛ لكونه بيع المنقول قبل قبضه، وكذا الاستبدال برأس مال السلم لا يجوز؛ لكونه يفوت القبض المشروط؛ لأن لرأس المال شبهاً بالمبيع، فلا يجوز التصرف فيه قبل القبض. (عيني، فتح) قبل القبض: أي قبل قبض المسلم إليه رأس المال، وقبل قبض رب السلم المسلم فيه. (عيني) بشركة: صورتها: رجل أسلم عشرة دارهم في كر حنطة، فجاء آخر إليه وقال له خذ مني خمسة دراهم وأشركني في الحنطة التي أسلمت فيها" فأشركه فيها لا يجوز. (مسكين) أو تولية صورتها أسلم رجل عشرة دراهم في كر حنطة، فجاء رجل آخر إلى هذا الذي أسلم وقال له: "بع" مني الحنطة التي أسلمت فيها بما أسلمت" فباعها بعشرة تولية لا يجوز، وكذا إذا باعها مرابحة على العشرة لا يجوز أيضاً؛ لأن المسلم فيه مبيع، والتصرف فيه قبل القبض لا يجوز، وفي التولية تمليك كله بعوض، وفي الشركة تمليك بعضه بعوض، فلا يجوز رمز الحقائق: ٧٦/٢] (فتح)
It is not valid to dispose of the capital or the salam commodity before receipt, whether by partnership, assignment, or sale at a profit, even if from the one who is obligated, before separation of bodies, i.e., separation of each of the two contractors in our view. (Ayni, Fath) And it is not valid to dispose, etc. Among the forms of disposal of the capital: to take something else in exchange for the capital, and also to gift the capital from the owner of the salam (rabb al-salam) before receipt. Among the forms of disposal of the salam commodity before receipt: to make another a partner in it, or to sell it, or to gift it before receipt. The reason for the impermissibility of disposal is that the salam commodity is a sold item, and disposal in a movable sold item before receipt is not permissible. The capital is due to be received in the session, and disposing of it forfeits that, so it is not permissible. The negated disposal includes sale, so even if the owner of the salam sells the salam commodity to the seller (muslam ilayh) for more than the capital, it is not valid. It includes substitution (istibdal), so if one substitutes the salam commodity with another type, it is not permissible because it is a sale of movable property before receipt. Likewise, substitution of the capital of salam is not permissible because it forfeits the stipulated receipt, since the capital resembles a sold item. Thus, disposal of it before receipt is not permissible. (Ayni, Fath) Before receipt: i.e., before the seller (muslam ilayh) receives the capital, and before the owner of the salam (rabb al-salam) receives the salam commodity. (Ayni) By partnership: its form: a man advances ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat; another comes to him and says, 'Take five dirhams from me and make me a partner in the wheat you have advanced for,' so he makes him a partner in it – it is not permissible. (Miskin) Or assignment (tawliyah): its form: a man advances ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat; another man comes to this one who advanced and says, 'Sell me the wheat you advanced for for what you advanced,' so he sells it to him for ten by assignment – it is not permissible. Likewise, if he sells it at a profit (murabahah) at ten, it is also not permissible. Because the salam commodity is a sold item, and disposal in it before receipt is not permissible. In assignment, ownership of the whole is transferred for a consideration; in partnership, ownership of part is transferred for a consideration, so it is not permissible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/76] (Fath)
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage expands on the general rule that disposing of either the capital or the salam commodity before actual receipt is invalid. The commentator enumerates specific forms: for the capital, taking something in exchange or gifting it; for the salam commodity, selling, gifting, or making another a partner. The underlying reason is that the salam commodity is analogous to a sold item, and the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibits selling movable goods before taking possession. Similarly, the capital in salam has priority for receipt in the session, and any disposal that forfeits that right is prohibited. The prohibition applies even if the disposal is a sale back to the seller, or a substitution. The passage clarifies that 'before receipt' means before the seller receives the capital and before the buyer receives the salam commodity. Examples are given: if a man advances ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat, and another person asks to partner by contributing five dirhams, this partnership is invalid. Similarly, selling the wheat on behalf of the buyer (assignment) before receipt is invalid. The relied-upon view is from Zahir al-Riwayah, and the general principle is rooted in the hadith: 'Do not sell what you do not possess.' The commentary also notes that the capital is treated as having a similarity to a sold item, hence the restriction.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession or delivery of an item; in salam, both capital and commodity must be received.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale by assignment where the seller transfers ownership at the original cost price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man advances 10 dirhams for a kurr of wheat. Before receiving the wheat, another person offers to pay 5 dirhams to become a partner in the wheat. According to the Hanafi school, this partnership is:
A) Valid because the capital has already been paid.
B) Valid if the partner is added before the seller receives the capital.
C) Invalid because the salam commodity cannot be disposed of before receipt.
D) Invalid only if the partnership is for profit, but not if it is a partnership in the wheat itself.

Key Takeaway
In salam, any disposal of the capital or the commodity before physical receipt is invalid (haram), whether by sale, gift, partnership, assignment, or substitution.

Iqalah of Salam: Prohibited Use of Capital before Receipt
Ruling on iqalah of salam and the dispute about using capital before receipt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on using the capital after iqalah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith and istihsan.

Core Idea
After cancelling a salam, the capital takes the ruling of a sold item: it cannot be used until received.

This lesson examines a specific dispute: after mutually cancelling a salam contract, may the capital owner immediately buy something from the seller with the capital owed? The Hanafi school prohibits it based on a hadith and the treatment of iqalah as akin to a sale, while Shafi'i and Imam Zufar permit it by qiyas. We will analyze the proofs and the Hanafi reliance on istihsan.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling of the Hanafi school: after iqalah, the capital owner cannot purchase from the seller with the capital before receiving it. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
The ruling is specific to a valid salam; a fasid salam has different rules.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that the Shafi'i school and Imam Zufar hold the opposite view: it is permissible to purchase with the capital before receipt because after cancellation the capital is a simple debt.
Imam Zufar is a Hanafi jurist who sometimes differs from the majority.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the qiyas argument: when the salam becomes void, the capital is a debt like any other. Exchanging a debt for goods is generally permissible, so buying with it before receipt is allowed.
This is a qiyas on ordinary debts.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi istihsan: the hadith 'لا تأخذ إلا سلمك أو رأس مالك' restricts options, and iqalah is treated as a sale in some respects, making the capital resemble a sold item. Thus, the same prohibition on selling before receipt applies.
Istihsan is a preferred ruling overriding qiyas due to a stronger evidence (the hadith) and the special nature of iqalah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi, the capital owner must first take possession of the capital before any new transaction; under Shafi'i/Zufar, he can immediately contract a new purchase.
This affects the timing and validity of subsequent sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تقايلا السّلم لم يشتر من المسلم إليه برأس المال شيئاً، ولو اشترى المسلم إليه أي رب المال كراً، وأمر لرب السلم بقبضه قضاء لم يصح، وصح لو قرضاً، أو أمره بقبضه له ثم لنفسه ففعل، ولو أمره رب السلم أن يكيله في ظرفه، ففعل وهو يشتري تقايلا السلم: بعد عقده ثم أراد رب المال أن يشتري برأس المال شيئاً قبل القبض لا يجوز وهو المراد بقوله: " لم يشتر". (عيني) لم يشتر: أي إذا تقايل رب المال والمسلم إليه السلم بعد عقده، ثم أراد رب المال أن برأس المال شيئاً بحكم الإقالة قبل القبض لا يجوز له ذلك، وقال الشافعي وزفر: يجوز له ذلك قياساً؛ لأنه لما بطل السلم بقي رأس المال دينا في ذمة المسلم إليه فيصح الاستبدال به كسائر الديون. وجه الاستحسان قوله علية: "لا تأخذ إلا سلمك أو رأس مالك" أي لا تأخذ إلا سلمك حال بقاء العقد، أو رأس مالك حال انفساخه؛ ولأن رأس المال أخذ شبهاً بالمبيع؛ لأن الإقالة بيع في حق غيرهما، ولا يمكن جعل المسلم فيه مبيعاً؛ لسقوطه بالإقالة، فتعين أن يجعل رأس المال مبيعاً، وإن كان ديناً في الذمة؛ لأن كونه ديناً لا ينافي أن يكون مبيعاً كالمسلم فيه قبل القبض، فامتنع الاستبدال قبل القبض، وهذا إذا كان السلم صحيحاً، أما إذا كان فاسداً، فلرب السلم أن يتصرف في رأس المال قبل قبضه. (عيني، فتح مسكين برأس المال شيئاً: بحكم الإقالة، حتى يقبض رأس المال، وهذا إذا كان السلم صحيحا. (ط، عيني)
If the two parties mutually cancel (taqayala) the salam, he (the capital owner) may not purchase anything from the seller (muslam ilayh) with the capital. And if the seller (i.e., the capital owner) buys a kurr and orders the owner of the salam to take delivery as settlement, it is not valid. But it is valid if as a loan, or he orders him to take delivery for him then for himself, and he does so. And if the owner of the salam orders him to measure it into his container, and he does so while he is purchasing. The meaning of 'they cancel the salam' is after the contract, then the capital owner intends to purchase something with the capital before receipt – it is not permissible, and that is the meaning of his statement 'he did not purchase'. (Ayni) 'He did not purchase': i.e., when the capital owner and the seller mutually cancel the salam after its contract, then the capital owner intends to purchase something with the capital by virtue of the cancellation before receipt – it is not permissible for him. Shafi'i and Zufar said: it is permissible for him by qiyas because when the salam becomes void, the capital remains as a debt in the liability of the seller, so substituting it (for a purchase) is permissible like other debts. The basis of istihsan is his statement (peace be upon him): 'Do not take except your salam or your capital.' That is, do not take except your salam while the contract remains, or your capital when it is rescinded. And because the capital acquired a resemblance to a sold item, since cancellation is a sale in respect of other than them, and the salam commodity cannot be made a sold item because it is removed by cancellation. So it is determined that the capital is made a sold item, even though it is a debt in the liability, because being a debt does not contradict being a sold item, like the salam commodity before receipt. Thus substitution before receipt is prohibited. This applies when the salam is valid. But if it is invalid (fasid), then the owner of the salam may dispose of the capital before receiving it. (Ayni, Fath, Miskin) 'With the capital' via the cancellation, until he receives the capital, and this is when the salam is valid. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage discusses the situation after the mutual cancellation (iqalah) of a valid salam contract. The core ruling is that the capital owner (rabb al-salam) cannot use the capital (which remains a debt) to purchase anything from the seller (muslam ilayh) before actually receiving that capital. The matn states: 'he does not purchase from the seller with the capital anything.' The commentator clarifies that this means after cancellation, if the capital owner wants to buy something from the seller with that capital, it is not allowed. The Shafi'i school and Imam Zufar disagree, permitting it by qiyas (analogy) because after cancellation, the capital is a debt like any other, and one can exchange debts. The Hanafi response relies on istihsan (juristic preference) based on the hadith: 'Do not take except your salam or your capital.' This means that during the contract, one takes the salam commodity; after cancellation, one takes back the capital. The hadith implies that the capital must be taken back as it is, not used for another transaction. Additionally, the Hanafis argue that the capital takes on the resemblance of a sold item because cancellation itself is treated as a sale in some respects. Since the salam commodity is no longer available, the capital is analogized to it, making it impermissible to exchange it before receipt. However, there is an exception: if the salam was fasid (invalid) from the start, then the capital owner may dispose of the capital before receipt, because the hadith does not apply to invalid contracts. The relied-upon position is that of the Hanafi school, based on istihsan over qiyas.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract; both parties agree to cancel the sale and return to the status quo ante.
إستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or equity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a valid salam contract is mutually cancelled, the capital owner wants to buy wheat from the seller using the capital that the seller owes him. According to the Hanafi school, this is:
A) Permissible because the capital is now a simple debt.
B) Permissible only if the purchase is for the same type of wheat.
C) Not permissible until the capital owner physically receives the capital from the seller.
D) Not permissible at all, even after receipt.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits using the capital after iqalah before receipt, based on the hadith and the principle of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Ruling on Seller's Purchase and Delivery as Settlement
Rules when the seller buys a kurr and asks the capital owner to take it as settlement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the seller in a salam contract buys a kurr and asks the capital owner to take it as settlement, and explain why it is invalid due to the requirement of two measurements, while distinguishing the loan analogy.

Core Idea
A salam settlement involving two transactions requires two separate measurements; otherwise it is invalid.

In salam contracts, payment is advanced for a future delivery of a specific commodity. When the seller himself purchases that same commodity from a third party and tells the capital owner to take it as settlement, a legal question arises: does this satisfy the debt? The Hanafi school rules it invalid because two distinct transactions—the seller's purchase and the settlement—are merged without proper measurement, violating a prophetic prohibition. However, if the original relationship is a loan rather than salam, the same method is valid, revealing a nuanced distinction.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the parties: the seller (muslam ilayh) owes a kurr of wheat to the capital owner (rabb al-salam). The seller then buys a kurr from a third party and asks the capital owner to take that kurr directly as settlement, without the seller having taken possession of it first.
2
Determine the ruling in the Hanafi school
The settlement is invalid (batil). The capital owner retains the right to demand the original salam debt.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling food until two measures are made: one by the seller and one by the buyer. This hadith is cited as evidence that each transaction requires its own measurement.
The hadith is ahad, thus zanni al-thubut, but accepted in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the reason: two transactions, two measurements
Here two transactions are combined: the seller's purchase from the third party (transaction 1) and the settlement with the capital owner (transaction 2). Each transaction requires an independent measurement. Since the capital owner takes delivery without the seller having measured after his purchase, the requirement is not fulfilled.
Think of each transaction as a separate sale; each sale of food requires measurement by the deliverer.
5
Contrast with the loan (qard) exception
If the original debt was a loan (qard) of wheat, then the same arrangement—the debtor buys a kurr and asks the lender to take it as settlement—is valid even without double measurement. This is because a loan is treated as a borrowing (i'arah), and the item taken is considered the same as the loaned item by estimation (taqdir), so two transactions are not realized.
This distinction shows the school's reasoning: in salam, the commodity is not yet owned by the seller; in qard, the loan creates a debt in the same genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى المسلم إليه كراً من الحنطة وكان رب السلم أسلم إليه في كر حنطة (عيني) بقبضه : أي بقبض ذلك الكر الذي اشتراه و لم يقبضه المسلم إليه (عيني) قضاء : أي لأجل القضاء عما عليه من الكر الذي هو المسلم فيه. (عيني) يهلك من لم يصح: أي لو أسلم رجل إلى آخر في كر حنطة، فاشترى الآخر كر حنطة، وأمر رب المال أن يقبض الكر الذي اشتراه مكان الكر المسلم فيه ولم يقبض المشتري الكر الذي اشتراه، لا يصح القضاء، حتى لو هلك بعد ذلك مال المسلم إليه المشتري، ولرب المال أن يطالبه بحقه؛ لأنه اجتمع هنا صفقتان: صفقة بين المسلم إليه وبين الذي اشتراه منه وصفقة بينه وبين رب السلم بشرط الكيل، فلا بد من الكيل مرتين؛ لنهيه عليها عن بيع الطعام حتى يجري فيه صاعان: صاع البائع، وصاع المشتري، ولم يوجد هنا، فلا يصح. (عيني، فتح) وصح لو قرضاً: أي لو أقرض زيد عمروا كرا من الحنطة ثم اشترى عمرو كر حنطة من بكر فأمر زيدا بقبضه قضاء لحقه فإنه يصح وإن لم يعد زيد الكيل؛ لأن القرض إعارة ولهذا ينعقد بلفظ الإعارة، ولو لم يكن إعارة لزم مبادلة الجنس بجنسه نسيئة وهو ربا، فكان المقبوض عين حقه تقديراً، فلم تتحقق الصفقتان فيكتفي مكيل واحد. [ رمز الحقائق: ٧٦/٢] (فتح) ثم لنفسه: أي لم يقبضه ثانيا لنفس رب السلم بأن يعيد الكيل ثانيا. (عيني) ففعل: أي المسلم إليه بأن كال له في ظرفه. (عيني) ففعل: أي رب السلم ذلك بأن اكتاله ثم اكتاله لنفسه. (ط، عيني)
And if the seller (muslam ilayh) buys a kurr of wheat (and the owner of the salam had advanced to him for a kurr of wheat) (Ayni) 'by taking it': i.e., by taking possession of that kurr which he bought and which the seller had not yet taken possession of. (Ayni) 'as settlement': i.e., for the purpose of settling what he owes of the kurr which is the salam commodity. (Ayni) 'Perishes from' 'not valid': i.e., if a man advances to another for a kurr of wheat, then the other buys a kurr of wheat and orders the owner of the capital to take possession of the kurr he bought in place of the salam kurr, while the buyer has not taken possession of the kurr he bought, the settlement is not valid. Even if after that the property of the seller-buyer perishes, the owner of the capital has the right to claim his due. Because here two transactions come together: a transaction between the seller and the one he bought from, and a transaction between him and the owner of the salam with the condition of measuring. So there must be two measurements, due to his prohibition (peace be upon him) regarding selling food until two measures are made: the measure of the seller and the measure of the buyer. And this did not occur, so it is not valid. (Ayni, Fath) And it is valid if as a loan (qard): i.e., if Zayd loans Amr a kurr of wheat, then Amr buys a kurr of wheat from Bakr and orders Zayd to take possession of it as settlement for his right, then it is valid even if Zayd does not repeat the measurement. Because a loan is a borrowing (i'arah) and therefore it is contracted with the word of borrowing. If it were not a borrowing, it would be an exchange of the same genus for itself on credit, which is riba. So what is taken is considered the same as his right by estimation, so two transactions are not realized, and one measurement suffices. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/76] (Fath) 'Then for himself': i.e., he did not take it a second time for himself, the owner of the salam, by repeating the measurement a second time. (Ayni) 'And he did': i.e., the seller did so by measuring for him into his container. (Ayni) 'And he did': i.e., the owner of the salam did that by taking it for himself and then taking it [again?] for himself. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: the seller (muslam ilayh) in a salam contract himself purchases a kurr of wheat from a third party, and then instructs the capital owner (rabb al-salam) to take delivery of that newly purchased kurr as settlement for the salam debt. The ruling is that this settlement is invalid. The reason given is that two separate transactions are involved: the seller's purchase from the third party, and the settlement between the seller and the capital owner. Each transaction requires its own measurement of the commodity, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited the sale of food without two measures: one by the seller and one by the buyer. Since the capital owner takes delivery directly without the seller having first taken possession and then measured again, the requirement is not fulfilled. However, if the arrangement is structured as a loan (qard), it becomes valid. For example, if Zayd had previously loaned Amr a kurr of wheat, and then Amr buys a kurr from Bakr and asks Zayd to take it as settlement of the loan, this is valid even without double measurement, because a loan is treated as a borrowing (i'arah) and the item taken is considered the same as the loaned item by estimation (taqdir). The commentary also clarifies that 'then for himself' means taking possession again for the capital owner's own account after the settlement, which would require re-measurement. The relied-upon position is clear: for salam settlement, two measurements are required; for a loan, one suffices.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Settlement of a debt; here, delivering the purchased kurr as payment for the salam debt.
قرض (qard): A loan of a fungible commodity that is repaid with its like.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract for a kurr of wheat, the seller buys a kurr from a third party and asks the capital owner to take it as settlement. According to the Hanafi school, this settlement is:
A) Valid because the capital owner receives the wheat he is owed.
B) Valid only if the seller first takes possession of the wheat.
C) Invalid because it violates the requirement of two measurements for separate transactions.
D) Invalid because the capital owner cannot accept anything other than the original salam commodity.

Key Takeaway
A salam settlement involving a purchased commodity requires two independent measurements because two transactions are present; otherwise it is invalid.

Buying with Capital After Cancellation in Salam: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Zufar
Hanafi ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on salam capital after iqaalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using salam capital after iqaalah, identify the opposing school's position, and explain the Hanafi istihsan-based response.

Core Idea
After cancellation, the capital is like a sold item — you cannot substitute it until you take possession.

This lesson examines a key dispute in salam contracts: after mutual cancellation (iqaalah), may the capital owner use the capital to buy from the seller before taking possession? The Hanafi school says no, based on a hadith and istihsan, while al-Shafi'i and the Hanafi scholar Zufar say yes, by qiyas. Understanding this ruling clarifies the role of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh and the special treatment of salam capital.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the parties
The issue is whether a capital owner (rabb al-mal) can buy something from the seller (muslam ilayhi) with the capital after cancelling the salam contract, before taking possession of the capital.
The capital is originally paid for the salam commodity.
2
State the Hanafi position and its primary evidence
The Hanafi ruling is that such a purchase is not permissible. The primary evidence is the hadith: 'لا تأخذ إلا سلمك أو رأس مالك' (Do not take except your salam or your capital).
The hadith restricts the options to only the salam commodity or the capital.
3
Identify the opposing position and their qiyas
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) argue by qiyas: after cancellation, the capital becomes a debt in the seller's liability, and substitution of debts is valid, so the capital owner may buy with it before receipt.
Zufar's disagreement is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response using istihsan
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to override the qiyas. First, the hadith explicitly prohibits substitution. Second, cancellation (iqaalah) is treated as a sale in some respects, making the capital akin to a sold item. A debt can be a sold item, as the salam commodity itself is a debt before delivery.
Thus, substitution before possession is invalid, just as selling a purchased item before taking delivery is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تقايلا السّلم لم يشتر من المسلم إليه برأس المال شيئاً، ولو اشترى المسلم إليه أي رب المال كراً، وأمر لرب السلم بقبضه قضاء لم يصح، وصح لو قرضاً، أو أمره بقبضه القضاء للمسلم إليه له ثم لنفسه ففعل، ولو أمره رب السلم أن يكيله في ظرفه، ففعل وهو. لأجل المسلم إليه أي المسلم إليه أي في ظرف رب السلم يشتري تقايلا السلم: بعد عقده ثم أراد رب المال أن يشتري برأس المال شيئاً قبل القبض لا يجوز وهو المراد بقوله: " لم يشتر". (عيني) لم يشتر: أي إذا تقايل رب المال والمسلم إليه السلم بعد عقده، ثم أراد رب المال أن برأس المال شيئاً بحكم الإقالة قبل القبض لا يجوز له ذلك، وقال الشافعي وزفر: يجوز له ذلك قياساً؛ لأنه لما بطل السلم بقي رأس المال دينا في ذمة المسلم إليه فيصح الاستبدال به كسائر الديون. وجه الاستحسان قوله علية: "لا تأخذ إلا سلمك أو رأس مالك" أي لا تأخذ إلا سلمك حال بقاء العقد، أو رأس مالك حال انفساخه؛ ولأن رأس المال أخذ شبهاً بالمبيع؛ لأن الإقالة بيع في حق غيرهما، ولا يمكن جعل المسلم فيه مبيعاً؛ لسقوطه بالإقالة، فتعين أن يجعل رأس المال مبيعاً، وإن كان ديناً في الذمة؛ لأن كونه ديناً لا ينافي أن يكون مبيعاً كالمسلم فيه قبل القبض، فامتنع الاستبدال قبل القبض، وهذا إذا كان السلم صحيحاً، أما إذا كان فاسداً، فلرب السلم أن يتصرف في رأس المال قبل قبضه. (عيني، فتح)
If the two parties cancel the salam, the capital owner does not buy anything from the seller with the capital. If the seller (i.e., the capital owner) buys a kurr and orders the capital owner to take possession of it as settlement, it is not valid. It is valid if as a loan, or if he orders him to take possession as settlement for the seller, then for himself, and he does so. If the capital owner orders him to measure it in his container, and he does so while [the capital owner] is absent, it is not possession, unlike the sold item. Commentary: "He does not buy": If the capital owner and the seller cancel the salam after the contract, then the capital owner wants to buy something with the capital by virtue of the cancellation before taking possession, it is not permissible for him. Al-Shafi'i and Zufar said: It is permissible for him by analogy, because when the salam becomes void, the capital remains a debt in the seller's liability, so substitution with it is valid like other debts. The basis of istihsan is his statement عليه: "Do not take except your salam or your capital" — meaning do not take except your salam while the contract remains, or your capital when it is dissolved. And because the capital takes the semblance of the sold item, for cancellation is a sale in respect of others, and the muslam fihi cannot be made the sold item due to its lapse by cancellation, so it is determined that the capital is made the sold item, even though it is a debt in the liability, for being a debt does not negate being a sold item like the muslam fihi before possession. Thus substitution before possession is prohibited. This applies if the salam is valid; if it is invalid, the capital owner may dispose of the capital before taking possession. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi ruling is stated: After cancellation (iqaalah) of a valid salam contract, the capital owner (rabb al-mal) cannot use the capital (ra's al-mal) to buy anything from the seller (muslam ilayhi) before taking possession of the capital itself. Al-Shafi'i and Zufar (a Hanafi scholar who differed here) allow it by qiyas, arguing that after cancellation the capital becomes a debt, and debts can be substituted like other debts. The Hanafi response uses istihsan: a hadith prohibits taking anything except the salam commodity or the capital. Further, cancellation is treated as a sale in respect of third parties, and the capital becomes akin to the sold item; since the muslam fihi (the commodity) is no longer existent, the capital takes its place as a 'sold item' even though it is a debt. A debt can be a sold item, as the muslam fihi itself is a debt before delivery. Hence, before the capital is actually taken into possession, it cannot be substituted. The ruling is for valid salam; if the salam was invalid, the capital owner may dispose freely.

Key Terms
تقايلا (taqayala): The mutual cancellation (iqaalah) of a contract by both parties.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital paid by the buyer (rabb al-mal) in a salam contract.
مسلم إليه (muslam ilayhi): The seller who receives the capital and promises to deliver the commodity.
استحسان (istihsan): Preferring a ruling based on a stronger evidence (here, a hadith) over a general analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, after cancelling a valid salam contract, can the capital owner (rabb al-mal) buy something from the seller with the capital before taking possession of the capital?
A) Yes, because the capital becomes a debt and can be substituted.
B) No, because the capital takes the ruling of a sold item and substitution before possession is invalid.
C) Yes, because the contract is cancelled and capital is free.
D) No, but only if the capital is not a measured item.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, after iqaalah, the capital owner cannot use the capital until he takes possession, due to the hadith and the capital's resemblance to a sold item.

Settlement of Salam Debt by Seller's Purchase: Valid Only with Loan or Two Measurements
Hanafi ruling on validity of settlement when seller buys same commodity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid methods of settling a salam debt when the seller purchases the same commodity, and apply the requirement of two measurements.

Core Idea
In salam, settlement via purchase of the same commodity is invalid unless the transaction is structured as a loan or two separate measurements are performed.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a specific scenario: the seller (muslam ilayhi) buys the same commodity (e.g., wheat) to settle the salam debt. The ruling distinguishes between an invalid purchase-based settlement and a valid loan-based or two-step measurement settlement. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper salam contract execution and avoiding riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) seller buys and orders capital owner to take possession as settlement (invalid), (2) seller buys and it is structured as a loan or with two measurements (valid). Identify the core act: settling a salam debt using a purchased commodity.
2
Understand the condition of two measurements
The reason for invalidity is that two separate transactions (seller's purchase and settlement) require two independent measurements (kayl) because of the prohibition against selling food without two measures. Recognize that this condition applies when food is involved.
Hadith: 'Do not sell food until two measures have passed: the measure of the seller and the measure of the buyer.'
3
Recognize the valid loan scenario
If the underlying transaction is a loan (qard) instead of salam, the settlement is valid without repeated measurement. This is because a loan is treated as borrowing (i'arah), and the received item is constructively the same as the right.
Loan avoids the need for two measurements because it is not a sale.
4
Recognize the valid two-step order scenario
If the seller orders the capital owner to first take possession for the seller (as if receiving the seller's purchase) and then for himself (the capital owner), the settlement becomes valid because the measurements are then properly performed.
This ensures two measurements: one for the seller's purchase, one for the settlement.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine whether the settlement method is valid by checking if two measurements occurred or if the transaction is a loan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى المسلم إليه كراً، وأمر لرب السلم بقبضه قضاء لم يصح، وصح لو قرضاً، أو أمره بقبضه له ثم لنفسه ففعل. لم يصح: أي لو أسلم رجل إلى آخر في كر حنطة، فاشترى الآخر كر حنطة، وأمر رب المال أن يقبض الكر الذي اشتراه مكان الكر المسلم فيه ولم يقبض المشتري الكر الذي اشتراه، لا يصح القضاء، حتى لو هلك بعد ذلك مال المسلم إليه المشتري، ولرب المال أن يطالبه بحقه؛ لأنه اجتمع هنا صفقتان: صفقة بين المسلم إليه وبين الذي اشتراه منه وصفقة بينه وبين رب السلم بشرط الكيل، فلا بد من الكيل مرتين؛ لنهيه عليها عن بيع الطعام حتى يجري فيه صاعان: صاع البائع، وصاع المشتري، ولم يوجد هنا، فلا يصح. (عيني، فتح) وصح لو قرضاً: أي لو أقرض زيد عمروا كرا من الحنطة ثم اشترى عمرو كر حنطة من بكر فأمر زيدا بقبضه قضاء لحقه فإنه يصح وإن لم يعد زيد الكيل؛ لأن القرض إعارة ولهذا ينعقد بلفظ الإعارة، ولو لم يكن إعارة لزم مبادلة الجنس بجنسه نسيئة وهو ربا، فكان المقبوض عين حقه تقديراً، فلم تتحقق الصفقتان فيكتفي مكيل واحد. [ رمز الحقائق: ٧٦/٢] (فتح) ثم لنفسه: أي لم يقبضه ثانيا لنفس رب السلم بأن يعيد الكيل ثانيا. (عيني) ففعل: أي المسلم إليه بأن كال له في ظرفه. (عيني) ففعل: أي رب السلم ذلك بأن اكتاله ثم اكتاله لنفسه. (ط، عيني)
If the seller buys a kurr and orders the capital owner to take possession of it as settlement, it is not valid. It is valid if as a loan, or if he orders him to take possession for him (the seller) then for himself (the capital owner), and he does so. [Commentary]: It is not valid: If a man pays salam to another for a kurr of wheat, and the other buys a kurr of wheat and orders the capital owner to take possession of the kurr he bought in place of the muslam fihi, and the buyer (seller) has not taken possession of the kurr he bought, the settlement is not valid. Even if after that the property of the seller-buyer is destroyed, the capital owner can claim his right. Because here two transactions are combined: a transaction between the seller and the one from whom he bought, and a transaction between him and the capital owner with the condition of measurement. Therefore two measurements are necessary due to the prohibition عليه of selling food until two measures have passed: the measure of the seller and the measure of the buyer. This did not occur, so it is not valid. (Ayni, Fath) And it is valid if as a loan: If Zayd lends Amr a kurr of wheat, then Amr buys a kurr of wheat from Bakr and orders Zayd to take possession of it as settlement of his debt, it is valid even if Zayd does not repeat the measurement. Because a loan is a borrowing (i'arah), therefore it is concluded with the wording of borrowing. If it were not borrowing, it would involve exchanging a commodity for its like on credit, which is riba. So the received item is considered the very item of his right constructively, thus two transactions are not realized, and one measurement suffices. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/76] (Fath) 'Then for himself': He did not take possession a second time for the capital owner himself by repeating the measurement. 'And he did so': The seller measured for him in his container. 'And he did so': The capital owner did that, i.e., he measured it then measured it for himself.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First: The seller (muslam ilayhi) buys a quantity of the same commodity (e.g., wheat) from a third party and orders the capital owner (rabb al-mal) to take possession of it as settlement for the salam debt. This is invalid because two separate sale transactions are involved (the seller's purchase and the settlement), and each requires its own measurement (kayl). The hadith forbids selling food until two measures have been performed. Therefore, the settlement is not valid; the capital owner still has the right to claim the original debt. Second: If the underlying transaction is a loan (qard) instead of salam, then such settlement is valid. The reasoning is that a loan is treated as a borrowing (i'arah), not a sale, so the property received is considered to be the same as the right without requiring new measurement. The passage also mentions an alternative method: if the seller orders the capital owner to take possession first for the seller (as if receiving the seller's purchase) and then for himself (the capital owner), it becomes valid because the measurements are then properly performed.

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A loan of fungible items where the borrower returns the same type and amount.
صفقتان (safqatan): Two separate transactions requiring independent measurements.
كيل (kayl): Measurement by volume for fungible goods like grain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract for wheat, if the seller buys wheat from a third party and orders the capital owner to take possession as settlement, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Valid, because the debt is settled.
B) Invalid, because two separate transactions require two measurements which did not occur.
C) Valid, only if the seller first takes possession of what he bought.
D) Invalid, but valid if the capital owner measures it again.

Key Takeaway
In salam, settling by the seller buying the same commodity is invalid unless the transaction is a loan or two separate measurements are performed.

Possession by Measurement in Seller's Container: Absent Capital Owner
Hanafi ruling on validity of possession when measured in the seller's container while capital owner absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when possession is valid in a salam contract when the capital owner orders measurement into his own container, and explain the distinction from a regular sale.

Core Idea
Possession in salam requires the capital owner to be present or take direct control; ordering measurement into one's container while absent does not transfer possession.

This passage clarifies a subtle point in salam contracts: the difference between ordering the seller to measure the commodity into your container when you are present versus when you are absent. The ruling hinges on whether the capital owner's right is a debt or ownership of a specific item. Understanding this distinction is crucial for valid possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage presents a case: the capital owner orders the seller to measure the muslam fihi into his (capital owner's) container, but the capital owner is absent. The ruling: this is not valid possession.
2
Analyze the legal basis
The capital owner's right is a debt in the seller's liability; he does not own the specific commodity until he takes possession. Therefore, his order does not encounter ownership and is ineffective.
3
Examine the container status
When the seller places the commodity into the capital owner's container, the seller effectively becomes a borrower of the container. He places his own property (his debt) in it, so no possession occurs for the capital owner.
4
Contrast with a regular sale (mabi)
In a regular sale, the buyer owns the specific item immediately upon contract. So his order to measure into his container is valid, and the seller acts as his agent, making possession valid even if the buyer is absent.
5
Identify the valid scenario
If the capital owner is present and the seller vacates (takhliyah) the commodity, possession is valid. Presence allows the capital owner to take control, and takhliyah completes the transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أمره رب السلم أن يكيله في ظرفه، ففعل وهو غائب لم يكن قبضاً بخلاف المبيع. لم يكن قبضاً [ أي لا يكون ذلك قبضا لرب السلم وحقه، وعليه أن يكيله ثانيا بحضرته. (ط، عيني)] أي إذا أمر رب السلم المسلم إليه أن يكيل المسلم فيه في ظرف رب السلم فكال المسلم إليه في ظرف رب السلم في حال غيبته لا يكون ذلك قبضاً من رب السلم لحقه؛ لأن حقه في ذمة المسلم إليه، ولا يملكه إلا بالقبض، فلم يصادف أمره ملكه فلا يصح، فيكون المسلم إليه مستعيراً للظرف، فجعل فيه ملك نفسه كالدائن إذا دفع كيساً إلى المديون، وأمره أن يزن دينه، ويجعله فيه، فإنه لا يصح، ولو كان رب السلم حاضراً، وكاله المسلم إليه بحضرته، وخلى بينه وبين الطعام، يصير قابضاً؛ لأن التخلية له، وفي بعض النسخ: "لم يكن "قضاء مكان "لم يكن قبضا" أي لا يكون أداء الحقه مطلقا، سواء كان فيه طعام لرب السلم أو لا . رمز الحقائق: ۷۷/۲] (فتح) بخلاف المبيع: أي إذا اشترى رجل براً معيناً، ودفع إلى البائع ظرفاً، وأمره أن يكيله، ويجعله في الظرف، ففعل البائع ذلك والمشتري غائب صح، ويكون قضاء لحقه؛ لأن المشتري ملك البر بنفس الشراء، فيصح أمره لمصادفته ملكه، فيكون قابضاً بجعله في ظرفه ويكون البائع وكيلا في إمساك ظرفه فيكون الظرف في يد المشتري حكما، فكان الواقع فيه واقعاً في يده حكماً، ولهذا اكتفى بذلك الكيل في الصحيح؛ لأن البائع نائب عنه في الكيل، ألا ترى أنه لو أمره بالطحن أو بإلقائه في البحر ففعل، يكون على الآمر في الشراء، وعلى المأمور في السلم، وليس ذلك إلا باعتبار صحة الأمر في الشراء وعدمها في السلم. فإن قلت البائع لا يصح أن يكون وكيلا له في القبض حتى لو وكله به نصا لا يصح توكيله ولا يكون قابضا له، فكيف يتصور أن يكون وكيلا له هنا؟ قلت لما صح أمره؛ لكونه مالكا له صار وكيلا له ضرورة، وكم من شيء يثبت ضمنا وإن لم يثبت قصدا وإن أمره المشتري أن يكيله ويجعله في ظرف البائع ففعل لم يصر قابضا؛ لأن المشتري صار مستعيرا للظرف عن البائع ولم يقبضه فلا يصح العارية؛ لأنها لا تتم بدون القبض فلا يكون الواقع فيه واقعا في يد المشتري فصار كما لو أمره أن يكيله وأن يجعله في ناحية من بيت البائع. (عيني، عناية)
If the capital owner orders him (the seller) to measure it in his (the capital owner's) container, and he does so while he (the capital owner) is absent, it is not possession, unlike the sold item. It is not possession: This does not count as possession for the capital owner of his right. He must measure it a second time in his presence. (T, Ayni) i.e., If the capital owner orders the seller to measure the muslam fihi in the capital owner's container, and the seller measures it in the capital owner's container while the capital owner is absent, that is not considered possession by the capital owner of his right. Because his right is in the seller's liability, and he only owns it by possession. His order did not encounter his ownership, so it is invalid. The seller becomes a borrower of the container, and he places his own property in it, like a creditor who gives a bag to a debtor and orders him to weigh his debt and put it in it – it is invalid. If the capital owner were present, and the seller measured in his presence and vacated for him the food, he becomes a possessor by vacating (takhliyah). In some copies, 'not qada' instead of 'not qabd', meaning it is not settlement at all, whether there is food for the capital owner or not. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/77] (Fath) Unlike the sold item: If a man buys specific wheat, gives the seller a container, and orders him to measure it and put it in the container, and the seller does so while the buyer is absent, it is valid and is settlement for his right. Because the buyer owns the wheat by the very purchase, so his order is valid as it encounters his ownership. He becomes a possessor by placing it in his container, and the seller becomes an agent in holding his container, so the container is constructively in the buyer's hand. Thus what is placed in it is placed in his hand constructively. Therefore, one measurement suffices in the correct view, because the seller acts as his deputy in measurement. Do you not see that if he ordered him to grind it or throw it into the sea, and he did so, it would be on the orderer in purchase and on the ordered in salam? This is due to the validity of the order in purchase and its invalidity in salam. If you say: The seller cannot be an agent for the buyer in possession; even if he explicitly appointed him as agent, the agency would not be valid and he would not be a possessor for him, so how can he be an agent here? I say: Because his order is valid due to his ownership, he becomes an agent by necessity. Many things are established incidentally even if not intended directly. If the buyer orders him to measure and put it in the seller's container, and he does so, he does not become a possessor, because the buyer becomes a borrower of the container from the seller and has not taken possession of it, so the borrowing is not complete without possession. Thus what is placed in it is not placed in the buyer's hand, so it is as if he ordered him to measure and put it in a corner of the seller's house. (Ayni, Inayah)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of possession (qabd) when the capital owner orders the seller to measure the salam commodity in his (the capital owner's) container while the capital owner is absent. The Hanafi ruling: It is not valid possession. Reason: The capital owner's right is a debt in the seller's liability; he does not own the actual commodity until he takes possession. His order does not affect his ownership of the container because the container is in the seller's hand and the seller puts his own property in it. The seller effectively borrows the container, and the food remains the seller's property. Thus the capital owner must repeat the measurement in his presence. The text contrasts with a regular sale (mabi') where if a buyer buys specific goods (like wheat) and gives his container to the seller to measure and place, the buyer becomes owner by the purchase itself, his order is valid, and the seller acts as his agent, so possession is valid even if the buyer is absent. This difference arises because in salam the subject matter is a debt, not a specific item, so ownership does not transfer until possession. The passage also addresses a potential objection: why can the seller be an agent for possession here when explicit agency for possession is not allowed? The answer: it is an incidental agency established by necessity. If the buyer orders measurement into the seller's container, it is invalid because the buyer does not own or possess that container.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): Container used for measuring or holding the commodity.
تخلية (takhliyah): Vacating the commodity so that the other party can take physical control; in Hanafi law, this constitutes possession (qabd) for moveables.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery, which transfers liability from seller to buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, if the capital owner orders the seller to measure the commodity into his own (capital owner's) container while the capital owner is absent, is this considered valid possession?
A) Yes, because the container belongs to the capital owner.
B) No, because the capital owner's right is a debt and his order does not meet ownership; the seller becomes a borrower of the container.
C) Yes, if the seller is honest.
D) No, but only if the container is not marked.

Key Takeaway
Possession in salam requires the capital owner to be present or have a valid agent; ordering measurement into his own container while absent does not transfer possession because the right is a debt, not a specific item.

Iqaalah with Slave-Girl as Capital: Effect of Her Death
Hanafi ruling on iqaalah with slave-girl as capital

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqaalah when the capital in a salam contract perishes before or after cancellation, and explain why the contract remains valid for cancellation.

Core Idea
Iqaalah is valid even after the capital perishes because the salam contract's subject matter is a debt in the seller's liability.

The salam contract is unique: the capital (ra's al-mal) is given upfront, but the subject matter (muslam fihi) is a deferred debt. What happens if the capital is destroyed before the parties cancel? This lesson clarifies that the cancellation remains valid, and the seller must compensate with the value of the capital at the time of taking possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage presents two cases: (1) the slave-girl dies after the parties cancel, and (2) the slave-girl dies before the parties cancel. In both, the iqaalah is valid. Determine what makes these cases similar in legal effect.
2
Locate the condition for iqaalah's validity
The passage states the condition: existence of the contract (wujud al-'aqd). Since the contract stands by its subject matter, and in salam the subject matter is the muslam fihi (a debt), the contract remains even if the capital perishes.
The capital is not the subject matter; it is the price paid.
3
Apply the condition to the cases
In both cases, the contract exists at the time of iqaalah because the muslam fihi (the debt of grain) remains in the seller's liability. Therefore, iqaalah is valid. The seller must pay the value of the slave-girl because returning the specific item is impossible.
The value is assessed on the day the seller took possession.
4
Contrast with a different scenario (optional)
The passage contrasts with a regular sale where the buyer owns the specific item immediately; there, if the item perishes, the contract may dissolve. In salam, because the subject matter is a debt, ownership does not pass immediately, so the contract persists.
This contrast highlights the unique nature of salam.

(عيني، عناية)
If a man pays a slave-girl as capital in salam for a kurr (of wheat), and the slave-girl is taken possession of (by the seller), then they cancel (the contract), and the slave-girl dies before the cancellation, the cancellation remains valid. If they cancel after her death, it is also valid. [Commentary]: He said: 'If a man pays a slave-girl as capital in salam for a kurr, and the slave-girl is taken possession of, then they cancel, and the slave-girl dies before the cancellation, the cancellation remains valid.' Then after her death they cancel, the cancellation is valid. And the seller must pay the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession in both cases. Because the condition for the validity of iqaalah is the existence of the contract, and the contract remains by the existence of the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh). The subject matter in salam is the muslam fihi, which remains in the seller's liability after the slave-girl perishes, so the iqaalah is valid from the beginning.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a slave-girl (amah) is given as the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract. The seller takes possession of the slave-girl. Two cases: (1) The parties cancel (iqaalah), then the slave-girl dies in the seller's hands before the capital owner takes her back. (2) The slave-girl dies first, then they cancel. In both cases, the cancellation is valid, and the seller must pay the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession. The reasoning: The validity of iqaalah depends on the contract still existing. The contract's subject matter is the muslam fihi (the commodity to be delivered), which is a debt in the seller's liability. The slave-girl is just the capital; her death does not affect the contract of salam itself. Therefore, the contract remains, and iqaalah can be performed. The seller compensates with the value because the capital (the slave-girl) is no longer extant.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave, given as capital in salam.
قيمة (qimah): The monetary value of the slave-girl at the time she was taken possession of by the seller.

C) The cancellation is valid, and the seller must pay the value of the slave-girl at the time he took possession.
D) The cancellation is valid only if the slave-girl died after the cancellation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, iqaalah in salam is valid even if the capital perishes, because the contract's subject matter is a debt in the seller's liability, and the seller must compensate with the value of the capital at the time of taking possession.

Iqalah in Salam After Destruction of Capital
Ruling on cancellation of salam when capital dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why iqalah in salam remains valid after the capital item is destroyed, and distinguish it from iqalah in a regular sale.

Core Idea
In salam, the subject matter is a debt; thus its destruction does not invalidate cancellation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a subtle but important point in Hanafi sales law: the effect of the destruction of the capital item (a slave-girl) on a cancellation (iqalah) of a salam contract. While one might think the destruction voids the cancellation, the Hanafi school rules it valid because the salam goods remain as a debt in the seller's liability. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and contrasts it with a regular sale, then outlines the four types of iqalah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal act is being discussed (iqalah in salam after the capital is destroyed) and the hukm level (sahih/valid).
Hukm level here is sahih (valid) – the cancellation remains effective.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the explicit reasoning given: the condition for valid iqalah is that the contract still exists, which in salam depends on the salam goods (muslam fihi) – a debt that survives the loss of the capital.
The proof is a clear talil: 'li anna shart sihhat al-iqalah baqa' al-'aqd...'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the condition applies: the contract remains because the subject matter (the salam goods) continues as a debt liability, unlike the capital which is not the subject matter.
4
Compare with the opposite case
Examine the contrasting scenario of a regular purchase: if the slave-girl dies, iqalah becomes void because she is the subject matter.
This contrast (wa 'aksahu) helps solidify the distinction.
5
Summarize the broader taxonomy
Note the four types of iqalah mentioned (salam, barter, sale of specific item, currency exchange) and their respective rulings regarding destruction.
For currency exchange (sarf), iqalah is valid even if substitutes are destroyed because the subject matter is an unspecified debt.

So she died in the hand of the one obligated before the owner of the salam had taken possession of her by virtue of the cancellation. (T, Ayni) It remained: The cancellation remains as it was when they cancelled and then she died. (Ayni) That is, if a man made a salam contract for a slave-girl for a kurr of wheat, and the one obligated took possession of the slave-girl, then they cancelled the salam contract, then the slave-girl died before the owner of the salam took possession of her by the cancellation, or she died before that and then they cancelled after her death – in the first case the cancellation remains, and in the second case the cancellation is valid after her death. And the one obligated must pay the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession of her in both cases; because the condition for the validity of cancellation is that the contract remains, and it remains by the existence of the subject matter and what is subject matter in salam – which is the salam goods – and it remains in the liability of the one obligated after the destruction of the slave-girl. So the cancellation is valid ab initio. He must pay her value. And its opposite is buying her for one thousand. And the saying is that of the claimant of poor quality and deferment, not of the denier. [Commentary on 'its opposite': its example is that he bought a slave-girl for one thousand, then they cancelled, then the slave-girl died in the hand of the buyer – the cancellation becomes void. If they cancelled after her death, the cancellation is also void; because the subject matter is the slave-girl, so the cancellation is not valid after her destruction ab initio, and destruction after it invalidates it and it does not remain due to the non-existence of the subject matter. So it is the opposite of the first. That is, cancellation in salam is like a barter sale (exchange of goods for goods) where cancellation is valid ab initio after the destruction of one of them and is not invalidated by it, because each of the two considerations is a subject matter as it is a sold item from one perspective, so the contract remains with the existence of one of them. The gist is that this type is divided into four sections: first, cancellation in salam; second, cancellation in barter sale; third, cancellation in sale of a specific item for a price; we have mentioned the rulings of these three; fourth, cancellation in currency exchange (sarf). Its ruling is that if they cancel after the destruction of one or both of the substitutes, or both or one of them is destroyed after cancellation before return, the cancellation is valid; because the subject matter in sarf is what is obliged for each of them in the liability of the other, which is unspecified, so its destruction is not conceivable, and what is possessed is other than it, so its destruction does not prevent the validity of cancellation. (Rawmuz al-Haqaiq 2/78) (Fath) With one thousand: the ruling in it is that if the slave-girl died before possession, the cancellation is void, and if the cancellation was after her death it is not valid. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of the destruction of a slave-girl (an item accepted in salam) on the cancellation (iqalah) of the salam contract. The author states that if the slave-girl dies after the cancellation but before the buyer takes possession of her, the cancellation remains valid. Similarly, if she dies before cancellation and then the parties cancel after her death, the cancellation is also valid. In both cases, the seller (muslam ilayhi) must pay the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession of her. The reason is that the condition for valid cancellation is the continued existence of the contract, which in salam is based on the salam goods (muslam fihi) – a debt in the seller's liability. The slave-girl is not the subject matter of the salam; rather, she is the price (or an item given as capital). Her destruction does not affect the contract because the salam goods remain in the seller's liability. The passage then contrasts this with a regular purchase of a slave-girl: if the slave-girl dies after cancellation, the cancellation is void, and if they cancel after her death, it is also void because the subject matter (the slave-girl herself) has perished. The author further categorizes cancellations into four types: salam, barter sale (muqayada), sale of a specific item for a price, and currency exchange (sarf). For sarf, cancellation is valid even after destruction of the substitutes because the subject matter is a debt in the liability.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): A mutual rescission or cancellation of a contract, restoring the original positions.
معقود عليه (ma'qud alayh): The subject matter of the contract; what the contract is based upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract for a kurr of wheat with a slave-girl as capital, if the slave-girl dies after the parties cancel the contract, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The cancellation becomes void because the slave-girl is destroyed.
B) The cancellation remains valid, and the seller must pay the slave-girl's value.
C) The cancellation remains valid, and the buyer forfeits his rights.
D) The cancellation is void, but the seller must still deliver the wheat.

Key Takeaway
In salam, iqalah remains valid after destruction of the capital because the subject matter is the salam goods (a debt), which survives.

Burden of Proof in Salam Disputes
Burden of proof in salam description/term disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that in a salam contract dispute, the claimant of a stipulated description or term is believed because he is the claimant of validity.

Core Idea
The claimant of validity in salam is believed due to the default that salam requires description and term.

In a salam contract, the goods must be described and the payment deferred. When parties dispute whether a specific description or term was stipulated, who bears the burden of proof? This lesson explains the Hanafi principle that the one claiming the stipulation is believed, because he asserts the validity of the contract, and the apparent circumstance of a Muslim is to avoid invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Determine whether the dispute is over the description (wasf) or the term (ajal) of the salam. The ruling applies to both.
2
Apply the principle 'al-qawl li-mudda'i al-sihhah'
The word is given to the one who claims validity. In salam, validity requires a specified description and a fixed term. Therefore, the party who claims that such a stipulation was made is the claimant of validity.
This is based on al-zahir (apparent circumstance) that a Muslim avoids invalid contracts.
3
Contrast with the denier
The denier of the stipulation is not believed because his claim would render the contract invalid (fasad), and the default is to uphold validity when possible.
Exception: if the claim appears to be obstinacy (ta'annut), it is disregarded.
4
Note the example given
If one party says 'we stipulated poor quality' and the other denies, the claimant is believed. Same if the claimant says 'we stipulated good quality' and the other denies.
The example illustrates that the ruling holds regardless of whether the claimed quality is good or poor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول لمدعي الرداءة والتأجيل، لا لنافي
المسلم إليه
الوصف والأجل.
=
وهو الرداءة
بخلاف
والقول لمدعي الرداءة [ بأن قال: هو شرطنا طعاما رديئاً، وقال آخر: لم نشترط شيئاً. (عيني)] الرداءة مثال، حتى لو قال أحدهما: "شرطنا جيداً" وقال الآخر: "لم نشترط شيئاً" فالحكم كذلك، وإنما كان القول لمدعي الوصف والأجل؛ لأنه مدعي الصحة؛ إذ السلم لا يجوز إلا مؤجلاً موصوفاً، فكان الظاهر شاهداً له؛ لأن الفاسد حرام والظاهر من حال المسلم تجنب الحرام والتأجيل أي والقول لمدعي التأجيل بأن قال هو: شرطنا التأجيل، وقال الآخر: لم نشترط شيئاً. (عيني) لا لنا في الوصف إلخ أي لا يكون القول لمنكر وصف المسلم فيه والأجل مطلقاً أيهما كان، والأصل فيه أنهما إذا اختلفا في الصحة والفساد فالقول لمدعي الصحة، فإن خرج كلام أحدهما مخرج التعنت كان باطلاً، القول قول من يدعي الصحة، وإن خرج كلام أحدهما مخرج الخصومة فكذلك عند أبي حنيفة إذا اتفقا على عقد واحد، وعندهما: القول للمنكر. ثم تفصيل المسألة: أن تقول: لو أسلم دراهم إلى أجل في كر حنطة، فقال المسلم إليه: شرطنا رديئاً، وقال رب السلم: = :
وكان
And the saying is that of the claimant of poor quality and deferment, not of the denier of the description and term to the one obligated. And the saying is that of the claimant of poor quality [as when he says: We stipulated poor quality food, and the other says: We did not stipulate anything. (Ayni)] Poor quality is an example. Even if one says: 'We stipulated good quality' and the other says: 'We did not stipulate anything', the ruling is the same. Rather, the saying is that of the claimant of description and term because he is the claimant of validity; since salam is not valid except with a term and description, so the apparent circumstance testifies for him. Because the invalid is forbidden, and the apparent condition of a Muslim is avoidance of the forbidden. And deferment: that is, the saying is that of the claimant of deferment, as when he says: We stipulated deferment, and the other says: We did not stipulate anything. (Ayni) Not for us regarding description, etc.: that is, the saying is never for the denier of the description of the salam goods and the term absolutely, whichever of them it is. The principle is that when they differ over validity and invalidity, the saying is that of the claimant of validity. If the speech of one of them emerges as obstinacy, it is void; the saying is that of the one claiming validity. And if the speech of one of them emerges as litigation, then likewise according to Abu Hanifa if they agree on one contract. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the saying is that of the denier. Then the detail of the matter: that you say: if he made a salam of dirhams for a term for a kurr of wheat, and the one obligated said: We stipulated poor quality, and the owner of the salam said: = : And was
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage deals with disputes in a salam contract regarding the description (wasf) and term (ajal). The ruling is that the burden of proof lies with the one who claims the existence of a specific description or a term, because he is asserting a condition that would make the contract valid. Salam is only valid if the goods are specified and the payment is deferred. Thus, the one who claims that poor quality was stipulated or that a term was set is claiming the validity of the contract. The apparent circumstance (al-zahir) supports him because a Muslim avoids invalid (fasad) transactions. The principle is that when parties differ over validity and invalidity, the word is with the claimant of validity. The passage also notes a difference within the Hanafi school: according to Abu Hanifa, if both parties agree on a single contract and one claims a stipulation while the other denies it, the word is with the claimant of validity. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the word is with the denier. The passage provides an example: if one says 'we stipulated poor quality' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', the claimant of poor quality is believed. The same applies if one says 'we stipulated good quality' and the other denies it. The passage ends mid-sentence on the page.

Key Terms
وصف (wasf): The description or quality of the salam goods, which must be specified for the contract to be valid.
أجل (ajal): The deferred time of delivery in a salam contract, a necessary condition for validity.
تعنت (ta'annut): Obstinacy or unreasonable claim; if one party's claim is clearly obstinate, it is disregarded.

Checkpoint

A) The buyer, because he is the denier.
B) The seller, because he is the claimant of validity.
C) Neither, the contract is void.
D) Both are believed equally, and the contract is nullified.

Key Takeaway
In salam contract disputes over description or term, the claimant of the stipulation is believed because he is the claimant of validity, unless his claim is obstinate.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf: Claimant vs Denier in Salam
Intra-Hanafi difference on burden of proof in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Abu Hanifa's position from that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the burden of proof in salam disputes and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: word to claimant of validity; Sahibayn: word to denier; difference stems from default presumption.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a difference of opinion on the burden of proof when parties dispute over a stipulation in salam. Abu Hanifa holds that the claimant of validity is believed, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the denier is believed. This lesson examines the basis of each view and which one is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the common scenario
Both parties agree that a salam contract was formed, but they differ on a specific stipulation (description or term). One claims it was agreed, the other denies.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's rule
Abu Hanifa: the word is given to the one who claims the stipulation, because he is the claimant of validity. The apparent circumstance (al-zahir) supports him since Muslims avoid invalid contracts.
This is the preferred position (mufta bihi).
3
Identify the Sahibayn's rule
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the word is given to the denier of the stipulation. Their reasoning: the default is that the contract is valid with the basic terms; an additional claim must be proven.
4
Evaluate the practical difference
The outcome of a dispute will depend on which view is applied. Under Abu Hanifa, the claimant wins; under Sahibayn, the denier wins.
The text indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is followed (by stating the general principle).
5
Note the exception for ta'annut
If a party's claim is clearly obstinate, it is disregarded regardless of the general rule.

The Saying in Disputes over Salam Conditions: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on who has the saying in salam disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for who has the saying when parties dispute over quality and term in salam, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In salam disputes, the saying goes to the claimant of validity, not the denier, unless the claimant is stubborn.

When a salam contract is disputed over conditions like quality or term, the Hanafi school has a nuanced rule: the saying (al-qawl) belongs to the one who asserts the validity of the contract, even if that person appears as the plaintiff. However, Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples differed on certain scenarios. This lesson unpacks the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The text states: 'if they differ over validity and invalidity, the saying is for the claimant of validity.' Recognise that this is the default Hanafi rule in contractual disputes.
The principle is derived from the apparent state of a Muslim avoiding invalid transactions.
2
Examine the stubbornness exception
If one party's claim amounts to stubbornness (ta'annut), his claim is rejected. The claimant of validity is presumed not stubborn.
A claim is stubborn if it contradicts a known benefit or apparent circumstance.
3
Apply to quality disputes
For poor quality: if seller claims stipulation of poor quality and buyer denies, seller's saying is accepted (buyer is stubborn). Opposite: buyer claims poor quality, seller denies – Abu Hanifa gives saying to buyer (claimant of validity), Sahibayn give to seller (denier).
The scenario where the buyer claims quality is the contested case.
4
Apply to term disputes
For deferment: if seller denies term and buyer asserts it, buyer wins by consensus (seller is stubborn). If seller claims term and buyer denies, Abu Hanifa gives to seller, Sahibayn to buyer.
Salam requires a term; thus denying the term is against the contract's validity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the contract is upheld with the claimed condition; under the Sahibayn, the contract is upheld without that condition.
This affects the obligations of the parties.

الإقالة، ولو تقايلا بعد موتها فالإقالة باطلة أيضا؛ لأن المعقود عليه فيها هي الأمة، فلا تصح الإقالة بعد هلاكها ابتداء وبالهلاك بعدها تبطل ولا تبقى؛ لانعدام المحل، فكانت عكس الأول، أي إقالة السلم بيع Upon him is its value, and its reverse is buying her for a thousand, and the saying is for the one claiming the poor quality and deferment, not for the denier of the description and term. Contrary to poor quality, and likewise it remains after destruction; because remaining is easier than beginning, so when the contract is rescinded, the one obligated to deliver must return the slave-girl, and if he is unable due to her death, then he must pay her value; because it stands in her place. Its reverse is buying her for a thousand: meaning the reverse of the mentioned ruling. The form: he bought a slave-girl for a thousand then they rescinded, and the slave-girl died in the buyer's hand, the rescission is void; and if they rescind after her death, the rescission is also invalid; because the object of contract is the slave-girl, so rescission is not valid after her destruction from the beginning, and destruction after it voids it and it does not remain; due to the non-existence of the subject. So it is the reverse of the first, i.e., rescission in salam is a barter sale – i.e., sale of goods for goods – where rescission is valid initially after the destruction of one of them, and it does not become void because of it; because each one of the two countervalues is a subject of contract; being a sale in one respect, so the contract remains with the remaining one. The gist is that this genus is divided into four categories: first, rescission in salam; second, rescission in barter sale; third, rescission in sale of a specific item for a price; and we have mentioned the ruling of these three. Fourth: rescission in currency exchange, and its ruling: that if they rescind therein after the destruction of one of the two countervalues or both, or both or one is destroyed after the rescission before repossession, the rescission is valid; because the object of contract in currency exchange is what is owed by each to the other in the liability, and that is not specified, so its destruction is inconceivable, and what is taken is other than it, so its destruction does not prevent the validity of rescission. And the saying is for the claimant of poor quality with one thousand: when one says: we stipulated poor-quality food, and the other says: we did not stipulate anything. Poor quality is an example; even if one says: we stipulated good quality, and the other says: we did not stipulate anything, the ruling is likewise.
