And term: meaning the saying is for the claimant of term: when he says: we stipulated the term, and the other says: we did not stipulate anything. Not for the denier of description etc.: meaning the saying is not for the denier of the description of the salam commodity and the term absolutely, whoever it may be. The principle in this is that when they differ over validity and invalidity, the saying is for the claimant of validity. If one's speech takes the form of stubbornness, it is invalid: the saying is of the one claiming validity. And if one's speech takes the form of litigation, so likewise according to Abu Hanifa when they agree on a single contract. And according to the two companions: the saying is for the denier. Then the detailed breakdown: you say: if someone pays dirhams on salam for a kurr of wheat with a term, and the seller says: we stipulated poor quality, and the buyer says: we did not stipulate anything, the saying is that of the seller; because the buyer is stubborn in denying validity. In its opposite: if the buyer claims stipulation of poor quality, and the seller denies the stipulation altogether, the saying is for the buyer according to Abu Hanifa; because he claims validity, and according to the two: the saying is for the seller because he is the denier. And if the seller says: it had no term, and the buyer says: it had a term, the saying is for the buyer by consensus; because the seller is stubborn in denying something that benefits him, i.e., the term, and it is a right, so it is invalid. In its opposite: if the seller claims the term and the buyer denies it, the saying is for the seller according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two: the saying is for the buyer; because he is denying a right against him, i.e., the term, and the saying is his.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses disputes in salam (advance payment sale) when the parties differ over whether a condition of quality or deferment was stipulated. The general principle is that the saying (al-qawl) belongs to the one who claims validity (siha) of the contract, because the apparent state of a Muslim is to avoid invalid transactions. The claimant of validity is not stubborn; the one who denies validity is considered contentious and his claim is rejected. The text details specific scenarios: if the seller claims poor quality was stipulated and the buyer denies any condition, the seller's saying is accepted because the buyer's denial amounts to stubbornness. Conversely, if the buyer claims poor quality was stipulated and the seller denies, then according to Abu Hanifa the buyer's saying is accepted (since he claims validity), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) give the saying to the denier. Similarly for disputes over the term. The passage also discusses the classification of rescission (iqala) in different types of sales: salam, barter sale, sale of a specific item for a price, and currency exchange. In salam, rescission after destruction of the object is invalid because the object is specific. In barter, it is valid if one countervalue remains. In currency exchange, rescission is valid even after destruction because the object of contract is an obligation in the liability, not a specific item.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl): The accepted statement in a dispute; which party's claim is given weight by the judge.
الرداءة (al-rada'a): Poor quality; a condition that the commodity is of inferior grade.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): Stipulation of a fixed term (ajal) for delivery in a salam contract.
المتعنت (al-muta'annit): One who is stubborn or contentious, making a claim that contradicts the apparent state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The seller, because he is the denier.
B) The buyer, because he claims validity.
C) The buyer, because he is the plaintiff.
D) The seller, because he is the one in possession.

Key Takeaway
In salam condition disputes, the saying goes to the claimant of validity unless he is stubborn; Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on which party is the claimant.

Validity of Istisna' by Ijma
Stating that istisna' is valid by ijma despite qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proof for the validity of istisna' (manufacturing contract) despite qiyas, and distinguish valid from invalid forms of istisna'.

Core Idea
Istisna' is valid by ijma (consensus) despite qiyas, based on the Prophet's own practice.

This passage addresses a classic Hanafi argument: istisna' (a contract for manufacturing a specific item) appears to violate the rule against selling non-existent goods. Yet the Hanafi school upholds its validity, relying on a powerful source: the continuous practice (ta'amul) of Muslims since the Prophet's time, which constitutes a binding ijma. We will explore the proof, the counter-argument by qiyas, and the distinctions between valid and invalid applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its core proof
What does the passage say about istisna'? What is the basis for its validity? The answer is 'ijma' (consensus) derived from continuous practice (ta'amul) since the Prophet's time.
The text also mentions the Prophet's commissioning of a seal and minbar as evidence.
2
Contrast with the qiyas argument
Why would qiyas reject istisna'? Identify the analogy: it is a sale of a non-existent item (bay' al-ma'dum), which is generally invalid. Note who held this view (Zufar and the three).
The Hanafis abandon qiyas here in favor of istihsan due to the stronger evidence of ijma.
3
Examine the two types of istisna'
Distinguish between istisna' in customarily traded items (valid) and those without customary dealing (invalid by consensus). Provide examples from the text (khuff, tast, qumqum vs. weaving a cloth or tailoring a shirt).
The criterion is whether people customarily engage in such transactions.
4
Understand the legal nature of istisna'
Resolve the internal debate: Is istisna' a sale (bay') or a promise (wa'd)? The correct view (al-sahih) is that it is a sale, and the subject matter is the specific item (al-'ayn), not the work. Evidence: the customer has an option (khiyar) to reject, and the contract becomes void with death, similar to ijarah.
This affects the rights and obligations of the parties.
5
Apply the rules to a new scenario
Given a modern manufacturing contract (e.g., custom furniture), determine if it qualifies as istisna' and whether it is valid. Check if there is customary dealing in such items.
Custom changes over time; contemporary practice may expand the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح السلم والاستصناع في نحو خف وطست وقمقمة، بالإجماع. أما السلم؛ فلأنه يمكن ضبط صفته ومعرفة قدره، فوجب القول بجوازه إذا اجتمعت شرائطه فيه، وأما الاستصناع فللإجماع الثابت بالتعامل من لدن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى يومنا هذا، وهو من أقوى الحجج، وقد استصنع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم خاتماً ومنبراً، والقياس أن لا يجوز؛ لأنه بيع المعدوم، وبه قال زفر والثلاثة، ولكن ترك العمل بالقياس بما ذكرنا. وصورة الاستصناع: أن يجيء إنسان إلى إسكاف فيقول له: اخرز لي خفين من أدريمك ويبين له صفتهما وقدرهما، ويبين الثمن، أو يجيء إلى الصفار ويقول له: اصنع لي آنية من صفر، ويبين له قدره وصفته ونوعه وجنسه وأجله ويبين الثمن ثم الاستصناع على نوعين: استصناع فيما فيه تعامل كما إذا استصنع في نحو خف، وهو جائز، واستصناع فيما ليس فيه تعامل وهو فاسد بالاتفاق، كما إذا طلب الحائك أن له ثوباً ينسج عنده، أو طلب من الخياط أن يخيط له قميصاً من كرباس من عنده، فهذا فاسد بالاتفاق. من الاستصناع وهو أن يطلب من الصانع أن يصنع له شيئاً بثمن معلوم. وطست وقمقمة إن كان يعرف بالوصف، وإن كان لا يعرف بالوصف فلا يجوز. الطست – بفتح مؤنثة أعجمية جمعها طاس وطوس، وقد يقال: "طسوت" ويجوز فيه السين المهملة والمعجمة، وفي "الصراح": "القمقمة " بضمتين كماسه يعنى كاسه، چوبين، أي يجوز السلم والاستصناع في مثل طست وقمقمة وغير الأواني فيما فيه تعامل الناس، وما لا تعامل فيها لا يجوز الاستصناع فيه، ولكن يجوز فيه السلم إذا اجتمعت شرائطه، والصحيح أن الاستصناع بيع لا عدة. وقال الحاكم الشهيد: إنه وعد وليس ببيع، وقيل: إنما ينعقد بيعاً إذا أتى به مفروغاً بالتعاطي، ولهذا يثبت الخيار فيه لكل واحد منهما، والمعقود عليه هو العين دون العمل عند الجمهور، وقيل: هو العمل، ولهذا يبطل بموت أحدهما، والأول أصح، ولهذا لا يشترط أن يعمله بعد العقد، حتى لو جاء به مفروغاً لا من صنعته، أو من صنعته قبل العقد، فأخذه جاز، وللمستصنع الخيار، وهو لا يثبت إلا في العين، وإنما يبطل بموت أحدهما؛ لأن له شبهاً بالإجارة من حيث أن فيه طلب الصنعة.
And salam and istisna' are valid in items like khuff (slipper), tast (basin), and qumqum (kettle), by consensus. As for salam: because its quality can be specified and its quantity known, so it is necessary to say it is permissible when its conditions are met. As for istisna': because of the consensus established by practice from the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to this day, and it is among the strongest proofs. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) commissioned the making of a seal and a minbar. By qiyas it would not be permissible because it is a sale of a non-existent thing, and Zufar and the three (Imams) said that, but action according to qiyas is abandoned due to what we mentioned. The form of istisna': a person goes to a cobbler and says: 'Make me two slippers from your leather' and describes their description and size, and states the price; or goes to a coppersmith and says: 'Make me a vessel of copper' and describes its size, description, type, genus, and term, and states the price. Then istisna' is of two types: istisna' in what is customarily practiced, like commissioning a khuff, and it is permissible; and istisna' in what is not customarily practiced, and it is invalid by consensus, like if a weaver requests that he have a cloth woven for him, or asks a tailor to sew a shirt from his own cloth, this is invalid by consensus. Istisna' is requesting the artisan to make something for a known price. And tast and qumqum: if they can be known by description, then it is permissible; if not known by description, then not permissible. Al-tast: with fatha, feminine, foreign, plural tas and tus; it may also be pronounced 'tasut'; both the unimportant sin and the marked sin are permitted. In al-Sirah: 'al-qumquma' with two dammas like a kasa (cup), i.e., wooden. So salam and istisna' are valid in such as tast, qumqum, and other vessels in what people customarily deal in. What has no customary dealing, istisna' is not valid therein, but salam is valid in it if its conditions are met. The correct view is that istisna' is a sale, not a promise. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said: it is a promise and not a sale. And it is said: it only becomes a sale when the item is brought finished by offer and acceptance; for this reason, each party has the option, and the object of contract is the specific item, not the work according to the majority. And it is said: it is the work, and for this reason it becomes void with the death of one of them. The first is more correct, and for this reason it is not required that he work after the contract; even if he brings it ready-made not from his own craft, or from his craft before the contract, and he takes it, it is permissible, and the customer has the option, and option is only established in a specific item. And it becomes void with the death of one because it resembles an ijarah (hire) in that it involves a request for craftsmanship.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the validity of salam (advance payment sale) and istisna' (contract of manufacture) in items such as khuff (slipper), tast (basin), and qumqum (kettle) by consensus (ijma). For salam, the basis is that it is a sale of a described item that can be precisely specified, so it is permissible when conditions are met. For istisna', the strongest proof is the continuous practice (ta'amul) since the time of the Prophet, who himself commissioned a seal and a minbar. Qiyas would suggest it is invalid because it is a sale of a non-existent thing (bay' al-ma'dum), and indeed Zufar and the three other imams hold that view. However, the Hanafi school abandons qiyas in favor of istihsan based on the established practice and the Prophetic example. The passage then describes the form of istisna' where a customer orders a specific item from an artisan at a stated price. Istisna' is divided into two types: (1) in items where there is customary dealing, such as footwear and vessels, which is valid; (2) in items where there is no customary dealing, such as ordering a cloth in the weaver's loom or a shirt from the tailor, which is invalid by consensus. The passage further discusses the nature of istisna': the correct view is that it is a sale (bay'), not just a promise (wa'd). The object of contract is the specific item (al-'ayn), not the work, although it resembles ijarah in involving a request for craftsmanship. This is evidenced by the fact that if the artisan brings the item ready-made from his own stock before the contract, the customer may accept it, and the customer has the option to reject it. Also, the contract becomes void if either party dies, similar to ijarah.

Key Terms
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract where a customer asks an artisan to manufacture a specific item from the artisan's own material for a known price.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the ummah; here, the continuous practice of Muslims since the Prophet's time is considered proof of consensus.
السلم (al-salam): Advance payment sale where the price is paid in advance and the commodity is described and delivered later.
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Customary practice of people; continuous dealing in such contracts over generations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi passage, what is the primary evidence that allows istisna' to override the qiyas that would prohibit selling a non-existent item?
A) Analogical reasoning (qiyas) on salam
B) The consensus (ijma) based on continuous practice from the Prophet's time
C) A direct Quranic verse permitting manufacturing contracts
D) The principle that necessity overrides prohibitions

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is valid by ijma (consensus) despite qiyas, due to the continuous practice of the Ummah and the Prophet's own example; it is a sale, not a promise, but only valid in items where customary dealing exists.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Validity of Salam and Istisna' and Disputes on Burden of Proof
Ruling on validity with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on validity of salam and istisna' by consensus, and apply the principle of obstinacy (ta'annut) to determine whose statement is accepted in disputes over conditions and term in salam.

Core Idea
The burden of proof shifts based on obstinacy, not merely on who denies.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq confirms the validity of salam and istisna' by consensus among Hanafi scholars. Then it delves into the logical intricacies of burden of proof when parties disagree on conditions or the term. Understanding these principles is essential for a mufti or judge to resolve disputes fairly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Note that salam and istisna' are valid by consensus for customary items like khuff, tust, and qumqumah. This is a foundational ruling.
This consensus is across the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the baseline principle
When no condition is stipulated, the muslim ilayh's (seller's) statement is accepted because the rabb al-salam (buyer) is being obstinate (mut'annit) by denying validity.
Obstinacy (ta'annut) is key to shifting the burden.
3
Analyze the first dispute on conditions
If the rabb al-salam claims a condition of poor quality and the muslim ilayh denies any condition, Abu Hanifa favors the rabb al-salam because he claims validity; the Sahibayn favor the muslim ilayh as the denier.
Abu Hanifa focuses on who is claiming the contract is valid.
4
Analyze the second dispute on term (muttafaq)
If the muslim ilayh denies a term and the rabb al-salam affirms it, both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree the rabb al-salam's statement is preferred because the muslim ilayh is obstinately denying something beneficial to him.
Unanimity here highlights the role of obstinacy.
5
Analyze the third dispute on term (mukhtalaf)
If the muslim ilayh claims a term and the rabb al-salam denies it, Abu Hanifa favors the muslim ilayh (the claimer), while the Sahibayn favor the rabb al-salam (the denier).
Abu Hanifa views the rabb al-salam's denial as obstinate because the term is a right for the buyer.
6
Synthesize the principle
The overarching principle is that the statement of the one who is not being obstinate is accepted. Obstinacy is determined by who is denying a right or benefit without valid reason.
This differs from the general rule that the denier's statement is taken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع باب السلم وصح السّلمُ والاستصناع في نحو خف وطست وقمقمة، بالإجماع لم نشترط شيئاً، كان القول قول المسلم إليه؛ لأن رب السلم متعنت في إنكاره الصحة، وفي عكسه بأن ادعى رب السلم شرط الرداءة، وأنكر المسلم إليه الشرط أصلاً، كان القول لرب السلم عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه يدعي الصحة وعندهما: القول للمسلم إليه ؛ لأنه منكر، ولو قال المسلم إليه : لم يكن له أجل وقال رب السلم له أجل، كان القول لرب السلم عندهم بالاتفاق؛ لأن المسلم إليه متعنت في إنكاره ما ينفعه، وهو الأجل، وهو حق فكان باطلاً، وفي عكسه بأن ادعى المسلم إليه الأجل، وأنكره رب السلم، فالقول للمسلم إليه عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: القول لرب السلم؛ لأنه ينكر حقاً عليه، وهو الأجل، وكان القول قوله [رمز الحقائق: ۷۸/۲] (فتح)
Book of Sales, Chapter of Salam. Salam and istisna' are valid in things like a khuff, a tust, and a qumqumah, by consensus. If we do not stipulate anything, the statement is that of the muslim ilayh, because the rabb al-salam is obstinate in denying validity. In the opposite case, where the rabb al-salam claims a condition of poor quality and the muslim ilayh denies the condition entirely, the statement is that of the rabb al-salam according to Abu Hanifa, because he claims validity; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the statement is that of the muslim ilayh because he is the one denying. If the muslim ilayh says: 'There was no term,' and the rabb al-salam says: 'There was a term,' the statement is that of the rabb al-salam according to all of them unanimously, because the muslim ilayh is obstinate in denying what benefits him, i.e., the term, and it is a right, so it is void. In the opposite case, where the muslim ilayh claims the term and the rabb al-salam denies it, the statement is that of the muslim ilayh according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them, the statement is that of the rabb al-salam because he is denying a right owed to him, i.e., the term, and his statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on the validity of salam (forward sale) and istisna' (manufacturing contract) by consensus, then details intra-Hanafi disputes on who bears the burden of proof when parties disagree. The passage is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the matn. The author begins by stating that both contracts are valid in customary items like khuff (leather sock), tust (basin), and qumqumah (flask), by consensus. Then, the commentary introduces scenarios of dispute: If no condition is stipulated, the muslim ilayh's (seller's) statement is taken because the rabb al-salam (buyer) is being obstinate by denying validity. In the reverse, if the rabb al-salam claims a condition of poor quality was stipulated and the muslim ilayh denies any condition, Abu Hanifa holds that the rabb al-salam's statement is accepted because he claims validity (i.e., the contract is valid with the condition), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the muslim ilayh's statement is accepted because he is the denier. Next, the passage addresses disputes about the term (ajal). If the muslim ilayh denies a term and the rabb al-salam affirms it, both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that the rabb al-salam's statement is preferred, as the muslim ilayh is obstinately denying something that benefits him. Conversely, if the muslim ilayh claims a term and the rabb al-salam denies it, Abu Hanifa favors the muslim ilayh's statement, while the Sahibayn favor the rabb al-salam's statement because he is denying a right (the term) owed to him. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that the burden of proof shifts based on who is being obstinate.

Key Terms
السلم (as-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a specified commodity to be delivered later.
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract to manufacture a specific item with the manufacturer providing both labor and material.
رب السلم (rabb as-salam): The buyer who pays the price in advance in a salam contract.
المسلم إليه (al-muslim ilayh): The seller who receives the price and is obligated to deliver the commodity in the future.
الأجل (al-ajal): The fixed term or deadline for delivery in salam or istisna'.
التعنت (at-ta'annut): Obstinacy or unreasonableness in denying a valid claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario do Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn unanimously agree that the buyer's statement is accepted?
A) When the buyer claims a condition of poor quality and the seller denies any condition.
B) When the seller denies a delivery term and the buyer affirms it.
C) When the seller claims a delivery term and the buyer denies it.
D) When no condition is stipulated and the buyer denies validity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates salam and istisna' by consensus, and in disputes, the party who is obstinate (mut'annit) loses the benefit of having his statement accepted, overriding the general rule that the denier is favored.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Hanafi Ruling on Istisna' (Manufacturing Contract)
Definition of istisna', its types, and its nature as a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istisna', distinguish its two types, and explain why it is valid despite being a sale of a non-existent item.

Core Idea
Istisna' is a valid sale by customary practice (ta'amul), overriding qiyas prohibition.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on the manufacturing contract (istisna'). You will see how the school draws on continuous practice from the Prophet's time to validate this contract, despite the general prohibition on selling non-existent items. We will also clarify the two types of istisna' and the debate over whether it is a sale or a promise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Istisna' is a contract to manufacture a specific item with the manufacturer providing material and labor. The ruling is that it is permissible (sahih) if it involves customary dealing (ta'amul) and all specifications are clear.
2
Locate the proofs
The Hanafi school bases this on the continuous practice (ta'amul) from the Prophet's time to the present, which constitutes a consensus. The Prophet himself commissioned a ring and a minbar.
This is the primary evidence.
3
Understand the qiyas objection
Qiyas would demand invalidity because it is a sale of a non-existent item (bay' al-ma'dum), and Zufar and the other three imams hold this view. However, qiyas is abandoned due to the stronger evidence of ta'amul.
4
Distinguish the two types
Istisna' is of two types: one with customary dealing (e.g., commissioning shoes) which is valid; and one without (e.g., asking a weaver to weave from his own cloth) which is void by consensus.
The second type is analogous to a sale without specification of subject matter.
5
Analyze the nature debate
The correct Hanafi view is that istisna' is a sale (bay'), not a promise (wa'd). This is supported by the existence of khiyar (option) upon seeing the item, the object being the item itself, and the fact that the manufacturer does not have to personally make it.
Al-Hakim al-Shahid held it is a promise, but the majority (including the author) choose the sale view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح السلم إلخ : أما السلم؛ فلأنه يمكن ضبط صفته ومعرفة قدره، فوجب القول بجوازه إذا اجتمعت شرائطه فيه، وأما الاستصناع فللإجماع الثابت بالتعامل من لدن النبي إلى يومنا هذا، وهو من أقوى الحجج، وقد استصنع رسول الله ﷺ له خاتماً ومنبراً، والقياس أن لا يجوز؛ لأنه بيع المعدوم، وبه قال زفر والثلاثة، ولكن ترك العمل بالقياس بما ذكرنا . رمز الحقائق: ٧٩/٢] وصورة الاستصناع: أن يجيء إنسان إلى إسكاف فيقول له: اخرز لي خفين من أدريمك ويبين له صفتهما وقدرهما، ويبين الثمن، أو يجيء إلى الصفار ويقول له: اصنع لي آنية من صفر، ويبين له قدره وصفته ونوعه وجنسه وأجله ويبين الثمن ثم الاستصناع على نوعين: استصناع فيما فيه تعامل كما إذا استصنع في نحو خف، وهو جائز، واستصناع فيما ليس فيه تعامل وهو فاسد بالاتفاق، كما إذا طلب الحائك أن له ثوباً ينسج عنده، أو طلب من الخياط أن يخيط له قميصاً من كرباس من عنده، فهذا فاسد بالاتفاق. (عيني، فتح) من الاستصناع وهو أن يطلب من الصانع أن يصنع له شيئاً بثمن معلوم. (عيني) وطست وقمقمة [إن كان يعرف بالوصف، وإن كان لا يعرف بالوصف فلا يجوز. (مسكين)] الطست – بفتح مؤنثة أعجمية جمعها طاس وطوس، وقد يقال: "طسوت" ويجوز فيه السين المهملة والمعجمة، وفي "الصراح" : "القمقمة " بضمتين كماسه يعنى كاسه ،چوبين، أي يجوز السلم والاستصناع في مثل طست وقمقمة وغير الأواني فيما فيه تعامل الناس، وما لا تعامل فيها لا يجوز الاستصناع فيه، ولكن يجوز فيه السلم إذا اجتمعت شرائطه، والصحيح أن الاستصناع بيع لا عدة. وقال الحاكم الشهيد: إنه وعد وليس بيع، وقيل: إنما ينعقد بيعاً إذا أتى به مفروغاً بالتعاطي، ولهذا يثبت الخيار فيه لكل واحد منهما، والمعقود عليه هو العين دون العمل عند الجمهور، وقيل: هو العمل، ولهذا يبطل بموت أحدهما، والأول أصح، ولهذا لا يشترط أن يعمله بعد العقد، حتى لو جاء به مفروغاً لا من صنعته، أو من صنعته قبل العقد، فأخذه جاز، وللمستصنع الخيار، وهو لا يثبت إلا في العين، وإنما يبطل بموت أحدهما؛ لأن له شبهاً بالإجارة من حيث أن فيه طلب الصنعة. (عيني، فتح، محشي)
«And salam is valid» etc.: As for salam, because its description can be specified and its amount known, so it is obligatory to say it is permissible when its conditions are met. As for istisna', due to the consensus established by practice from the Prophet's time to our own, which is among the strongest proofs. The Prophet ﷺ commissioned a ring and a minbar. Analogy (qiyas) would indicate that it is not permissible because it is a sale of a non-existent item, and Zufar and the three (other imams) say so, but qiyas is abandoned by what we have mentioned. The form of istisna': that a person comes to a shoemaker and says: 'Stitch for me two khuffs from your leather' and specifies their description and measure, and specifies the price; or comes to a brass-worker and says: 'Make for me a vessel from brass' and specifies its measure, description, type, kind, and term, and specifies the price. Then istisna' is of two types: istisna' in what has customary dealing (ta'amul), like commissioning a khuff, which is permissible; and istisna' in what has no customary dealing, which is void by consensus, like if one asks a weaver to weave a cloth from his own material, or asks a tailor to sew a shirt from his own cloth, this is void by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) Istisna' is that one asks the craftsman to make something for a fixed price. (Ayni) And 'tust' and 'qumqumah' [if they can be known by description, they are permissible; if not known by description, not permissible. (Miskin)] The tust (basin) is a foreign feminine noun, plural tas and tus, and it is said 'tasut'; the letter sin may be unpointed or pointed. In al-Sirah: 'al-qumqumah' with two dammas means a flask. So salam and istisna' are permissible in such items as tust and qumqumah and other utensils in what has customary dealing, and what has no customary dealing, istisna' is not permissible, but salam is permissible if its conditions are met. The correct view is that istisna' is a sale, not a promise. Al-Hakim al-Shahid said it is a promise and not a sale. Some say it is only concluded as a sale when the item is delivered complete by taking (ta'ati). For this reason, the option (khiyar) is established for each of them. The object of contract is the item itself, not the work, according to the majority. Some say it is the work, and for this reason it becomes void with the death of one party. The first is more correct, and for this reason it is not required that he make it after the contract; even if he brings it ready-made not from his own manufacture, or from his own manufacture before the contract, and he takes it, it is permissible. The mustasni' (customer) has the option, which is only established over the item. It becomes void with the death of one party because it has similarity to ijarah (hire) in that it involves requesting work. (Ayni, Fath, Mahshi)
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of istisna' (manufacturing contract) as a follow-up to the earlier ruling on its validity. The commentary begins by explaining the rationale for the ruling: salam is valid because its specifications can be defined, while istisna' is validated by continuous practice (ta'amul) from the Prophet's time onward, which constitutes a strong proof. The author notes that qiyas (analogy) would reject istisna' as it is a sale of a non-existent item (bay' al-ma'dum), and indeed Zufar and the three other imams hold this view, but the Hanafi school abandons qiyas in favor of the practice-based consensus. The passage then defines the form (surah) of istisna' with examples: a customer goes to a shoemaker or a brass-worker, specifies the item, its description, and price. It then categorizes istisna' into two types: one with customary dealing (like khuffs) which is permissible, and one without (like asking a weaver to weave from his own cloth) which is void by consensus. The text also clarifies the linguistic meanings of 'tust' and 'qumqumah'. A key point is the nature of istisna': the correct Hanafi view (al-sahih) is that it is a sale (bay'), not a mere promise (wa'd). Al-Hakim al-Shahid held it is a promise. The author supports the sale view with several consequences: khiyar (option) is established for both parties, the object of contract is the item not the work, and it does not require the manufacturer to personally make it. The passage concludes by noting that the contract becomes void with the death of one party due to its resemblance to ijarah.

Key Terms
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract to manufacture a specific item with the manufacturer providing material and labor.
التعامل (at-ta'amul): Customary practice and dealing among people that serves as evidence for legal rulings.
بيع المعدوم (bay' al-ma'dum): Sale of a non-existent item, generally prohibited in Hanafi law except in salam and istisna'.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option to rescind or confirm a contract upon seeing the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone commissions a weaver to weave a cloth from the weaver's own material?
A) Valid, because it is istisna' with customary dealing
B) Void by consensus
C) Permissible only if the price is paid in advance
D) Valid with khiyar al-ru'ya

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a valid sale by customary practice; it is void if the item lacks customary dealing; the customer has an option upon seeing the item.

Khiyar al-Ru'ya in Istisna' (Option of Inspection)
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya for mustasni' and manufacturer in istisna'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who has the option of inspection in an istisna' contract according to the preferred Hanafi view and explain the reasoning of the differing imams.

Core Idea
In istisna', only the customer has the option of inspection; the manufacturer does not, though Abu Hanifa differed.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the right of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) in an istisna' contract. Istisna' is a unique contract for manufacturing goods, and the issue is whether the buyer may reject the item after seeing it. The preferred Hanafi position limits the option to the customer, but there is disagreement among the early imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage discusses the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) in an istisna' contract. The ruling is that the customer has the option, but the manufacturer does not according to the preferred view.
2
Trace the three positions
Identify the views: (a) al-sahih: only customer; (b) Abu Hanifa: both have option; (c) Abu Yusuf: neither has option.
The text mentions Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's views explicitly.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each
For al-sahih: customer bought unseen; manufacturer sold unseen. For Abu Hanifa: manufacturer suffers harm from wasted materials. For Abu Yusuf: manufacturer expended his property, so customer's option would harm him.
4
Note the tarjih
The author states 'al-sahih' for the customer-only view, indicating it is the preferred position in the school.
5
Apply to a real scenario
Consider a custom shoe order. The customer sees the shoes and is dissatisfied. Under the preferred view, he may reject them; the manufacturer cannot reject the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله الخيار إذا رآه [ أي وإذا عمله الصانع للمستصنع فللمستصنع له خيار الرؤية. (معدن)] أي للمستصنع الخيار بعد رؤية المصنوع إن شاء أخذه وإن شاء تركه؛ لأنه اشترى ما لم يره بخلاف السلم؛ لأنه دين في الذمة، ولا خيار للصانع؛ لأنه باع ما لم يره، وهو الأصح بناء على جعله بيعا لا عدة، وعن أبي حنيفة: أن له الخيار أيضاً؛ لأنه يلحقه الضرر بقطع الصرم وإتلاف الخيط ونحوه. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا خيار لواحد منهما، أما للصانع فلأنه بائع، ولا خيار لمن باع ما لم يره، وأما المستصنع فلأن الصانع أتلف ماله ليصل إلى بدله، فلو ثبت له الخيار تضرر الصانع فربما لا يرغب فيه غير المستصنع، والصحيح أن للمستصنع الخيار دون الصانع رمز الحقائق: ٧٩/٢] (فتح، عناية) وللصانع بيعه إلخ لأنه لا يتعين إلا باختيار المستصنع، وإنما قيد بـ "قبل أن يراه"؛ لأنه إذا رآه واختاره صار متعينا ولم يبق للصانع فيه حق البيع، فليس له أن يبيعه؛ لأنه بالإحضار أسقط خياره ولزم من جانبه، فإذا رضي به المستصنع ثبت اللزوم في حقه أيضاً. (عيني، فتح) ومؤجله سلم: أي إذا ضرب الأجل في الاستصناع الذي فيه تعامل فهو سلم عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: هو استصناع؛ لأنهما عقدان مختلفان اسماً وحكماً، فلا ينعقد أحدهما بالآخر، وله أنه أمكن تصحيحه سلماً وأمكن تصحيحه استصناعاً، وجعله سلماً أولى؛ لأنه عقد جاءت به السنة والاستصناع ثبت بتعامل الناس لا بالحديث أي بقول النبي عليه ، وأما ما ثبت بفعله فهو غريب، والمراد من الأجل ما يصلح أن يكون في السلم، وهو الشهر كما مر، وإن ضرب الأجل في الاستصناع الذي لا تعامل فيه فهو سلم بالاتفاق؛ لأن الاستصناع فيه لا يجوز إجماعاً، فتعين حمله على السلم تحرياً للجواز، وإنما الخلاف فيما فيه تعامل كالخف ونحوه؛ لأنه يحتمل الوجهين. وفائدة الخلاف : أنه يشترط عنده جميع شرائط السلم كقبض رأس المال واستقصاء الوصف وتعيينه قبل الافتراق وغيره، ولا يكون له خيار رؤية وإن كان الأجل أقل كان استصناعاً إن جرى فيه التعامل، وإلا ففاسد، وهذا إذا ذكر الأجل على سبيل الاستمهال أي تأخير المطالبة بالتسليم، وإن ذكره على وجه الاستعجال بأن قال: على غداً أو بعد غد يكون استصناعاً؛ لأن ذكر الأجل حينئذ للفراغ لا لتأخير المطالبة، وقيل: إن ذكر أدنى مدة يتمكن فيها من العمل فهو استصناع، وإن كان أكثر من ذلك فهو سلم، وفصل الهندواني: أن ذكر الأجل إن كان من قبل المستصنع فهو للاستعجال، فلا يصير سلماً، وإن كان من قبل الصانع فهو للاستمهال، فيكون سلماً. رمز الحقائق: ۸۰/۲] (فتح) أن تفرغ عنه
«He has the option when he sees it» [i.e., when the manufacturer makes it for the customer, the customer has the option of inspection. (Ma'dan)] That is, the mustasni' has the option after seeing the manufactured item: if he wishes he may take it, and if he wishes he may leave it, because he bought what he did not see, unlike salam because it is a debt in the dhimmah. The manufacturer has no option because he sold what he did not see, and that is the most correct based on making it a sale not a promise. According to Abu Hanifa, he also has the option because he suffers harm by cutting the leather and wasting thread etc. According to Abu Yusuf, neither has an option: as for the manufacturer, because he is a seller and one who sells what he did not see has no option; as for the mustasni', because the manufacturer has expended his property to receive its substitute, so if the option were established for him it would harm the manufacturer, and perhaps none other than the mustasni' would desire it. The correct view is that the mustasni' has the option, not the manufacturer. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 79/2] (Fath, Inayah) «And for the manufacturer, his sale» etc, because it is not determined except by the choice of the mustasni'. It is restricted to «before he sees it» because if he sees it and chooses it, it becomes determined and the manufacturer no longer has the right to sell it; thus he cannot sell it because by presenting it he has dropped his option and it becomes binding on his part, and when the mustasni' is pleased with it, binding is established on his side too. (Ayni, Fath) «And its term is salam»: That is, if a term is set in istisna' that has customary dealing, it is salam according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: it is istisna', because they are two different contracts in name and ruling, so one cannot be concluded by the other. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that it can be validated either as salam or as istisna', and making it salam is better because it is a contract established by Sunnah, while istisna' is established by practice, not hadith. As for what is established by action (fi'l), it is rare. The intended term is what is suitable for salam, which is a month as previously. If a term is set in istisna' that has no customary dealing, it is salam by consensus, because istisna' is not permissible in it, so it is necessarily construed as salam to seek permissibility. The disagreement is only in what has customary dealing like khuff etc., because it admits both possibilities. The benefit of the disagreement is that according to him (Abu Hanifa), all conditions of salam are required, like payment of the principal, complete description, specification before separation, etc., and there is no option of inspection. If the term is less, it is istisna' if there is customary dealing, otherwise void. This is when the term is mentioned for respite (istimhal), i.e., deferment of delivery. If it is mentioned for urgency (isti'jal), like saying 'by tomorrow' or 'day after', it is istisna', because then the term is for completion, not deferment. Some say: if a minimal period sufficient for the work is mentioned, it is istisna'; if more than that, it is salam. Al-Hinduwani distinguished: if the term is mentioned by the mustasni', it is for urgency, so it does not become salam; if by the manufacturer, it is for respite, so it becomes salam. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 80/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage discusses two important issues in istisna': the right of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) and the effect of setting a term. The author begins by confirming that the mustasni' (customer) has the option to inspect the manufactured item and either accept or reject it, because he bought something unseen. The manufacturer generally has no such option because he sold what he did not see — this is the most correct view based on considering istisna' a sale. However, Abu Hanifa is reported to give the manufacturer an option too, due to potential harm from cutting materials. Abu Yusuf denies any option to either party. The preferred view (al-sahih) is that only the customer has the option. The passage then explains that once the customer sees and accepts the item, it becomes binding, and the manufacturer cannot sell it to another. The second issue concerns the term: if a term (ajal) is set in an istisna' contract that involves customary dealing, Abu Hanifa considers it a salam contract, requiring all conditions of salam (such as advance payment of price, full specification, no option of inspection). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it still istisna', as they are distinct contracts. In non-customary items, setting a term makes it salam by consensus. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that in Abu Hanifa's view, the strict salam conditions apply, including no khiyar al-ru'ya. The passage also provides nuanced rules: if the term is very short (like a day or two), it remains istisna' because it is for urgency. Al-Hinduwani adds that the identity of the party setting the term (customer vs. manufacturer) determines whether it is for urgency or respite.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar ar-ru'ya): The option to rescind or confirm a contract after seeing the item purchased unseen.
المستصنع (al-mustasni'): The customer who orders the manufacturing of an item.
الصانع (as-sani'): The manufacturer/craftsman in an istisna' contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (al-sahih), who has the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) in an istisna' contract?
A) Only the customer (mustasni')
B) Only the manufacturer (sani')
C) Both have the option
D) Neither has the option

Key Takeaway
In istisna', only the customer has the option to reject the item after seeing it; the manufacturer must accept the customer's decision.

Effect of Setting a Term in Istisna' (Does it Become Salam?)
Effect of term on istisna' becoming salam, with intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that setting a term in istisna' may convert it into a salam contract, and determine when it remains istisna' based on the intention and customary dealing.

Core Idea
If a term is set in a customary istisna' contract, Abu Hanifa considers it salam; the Sahibayn consider it still istisna'.

This passage continues from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the effect of specifying a delivery term in an istisna' contract. Istisna' is typically open-ended, but if a term is set, it might become a salam contract, which has stricter conditions. The Hanafi founders differ on this, with Abu Hanifa leaning towards validation as salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that we are dealing with an istisna' contract where a term (ajal) is set, and the item has customary dealing (like khuff).
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: it becomes salam. Sahibayn: it remains istisna'.
Also note that for non-customary items, it is salam by consensus.
3
Examine the proof for each
Abu Hanifa: validation as salam is possible and preferable because salam is based on Sunnah. Sahibayn: the two contracts are distinct and cannot be mixed.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, all salam conditions apply (advance payment, no khiyar al-ru'ya). Under Sahibayn, the contract remains istisna' with its usual rules.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Apply the nuances
Consider the length of term, who sets it, and whether it is for respite (istimhal) or urgency (isti'jal). According to some, a very short term keeps it as istisna'.
Al-Hinduwani further distinguishes based on who sets the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الأجل (al-ajal): The deferred term for delivery in salam or istisna'.
الاستمهال (al-istimhal): Requesting respite or deferment of delivery.
الاستعجال (al-isti'jal): Requesting urgency or quick completion.
السلم (as-salam): A forward contract with advance payment, based on Sunnah.
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract for custom manufacturing, based on custom.

According to Abu Hanifa, what happens if a term (ajal) is set in an istisna' contract that involves customary dealing?
A) The contract becomes salam and all salam conditions apply.

Key Takeaway
Setting a term in a customary istisna' contract may convert it into salam according to Abu Hanifa, eliminating the customer's option of inspection and requiring advance payment.

The Sale of Dogs and Other Animals in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on sale of dogs, predators, birds, and dhimmis' transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling dogs and predatory animals, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on vicious dogs, and apply the principle that lawful benefit makes an object valuable property.

Core Idea
A sale is valid if the object is beneficial – Hanafi permits dog sales based on the exception for hunting and guarding.

This passage from the 'Chapter of Miscellaneous Matters' (Bab al-Mutafarriqat) deals with several rulings that do not fit neatly into earlier sales chapters. The central issue is the permissibility of selling dogs and other predatory animals. The Hanafi school argues that such sales are valid as long as the animal provides lawful benefit, even if it is a dog. The text also discusses the status of dhimmis in transactions, affirming their full legal capacity except for wine and pork.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the Hanafi ruling
The act is the sale of a dog, cheetah, predators, birds, etc. The Hanafi ruling is that these sales are valid (sahih) because the animals are valuable property and provide lawful benefit.
Note the exception: pigs and harmful vermin are not salable.
2
Locate the dalil for the permissibility of dog sales
The primary evidence is the hadith from Ibn 'Abbas: the Prophet ﷺ forbade the sale of dogs except for hunting dogs or herd dogs (al-Daraqutni). This exception indicates that beneficial dogs are allowed.
The Hanafis interpret the general prohibition hadith ('the price of a dog is unlawful earnings') as abrogated or restricted to the early period of Islam.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof establishes the ruling)
The exception in the hadith shows that dogs with a lawful purpose (hunting, guarding, herding) are beneficial and thus valuable property. The Hanafi principle is that anything with lawful benefit is mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) and can be sold.
Even an untrained dog provides benefit by guarding and barking, so its sale is valid.
4
Compare with the opposing school (Shafi'i/Hanbali)
The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that the sale of all dogs is invalid based on the hadith 'the price of a dog is unlawful earnings.' They do not accept the exception as strong enough to override the general prohibition.
The Hanafi response is that the exception hadith is specific and must be given precedence over the general prohibition.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the sale is valid (Hanafi), the dog is guaranteed property; if someone destroys it, they must pay its value. If the sale is invalid (Shafi'i), the dog is not property, and no guarantee applies.
This difference also affects whether a Muslim can buy a dog for security or hunting from a non-Muslim seller.

Chapter of Miscellaneous matters. The sale of a dog, cheetah, predators, birds, and a dhimmi is valid like a Muslim in the sale of other than wine... whether you know it or not, from the chapters. Chapter of Miscellaneous: i.e., explaining its rulings from various chapters. ('Ayni) It was the habit of authors to mention what is unusual at the end of the book. (Miskin) The sale of the dog because it is valuable property, an instrument for hunting like the falcon. According to al-Shafi'i: its sale is not permissible at all due to his prohibition of the dog, saying: 'Indeed from the unlawful earnings is the dowry of a prostitute and the price of a dog,' and Ahmad said the same. And for us: what is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet ﷺ forbade the sale of a dog except a hunting dog or a herd dog, reported by al-Daraqutni. And in a narration: 'except a dog that is predatory and trained.' And what is narrated is interpreted as pertaining to the beginning of Islam when he had ordered killing dogs. And there is no difference in that among all types of dogs, trained and untrained; because the trained one is suitable for sale due to being beneficial actually and legally, so it is property. As for the untrained, it is benefited from in other than hunting, for every dog guards its owner's house, prevents strangers from entering his house, and informs about the coming one by its barking, so it equals the trained in benefit. Shams al-A'imma stipulated for permissibility that it be trained or capable of training. From Abu Yusuf: the sale of a vicious dog that does not accept training is not valid because it is not benefited from, so it becomes like harmful vermin, and this is the correct in the madhhab. And if a man destroys another's dog, he guarantees its value with us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Ramiż al-Haqa'iq 2/80 (Fath, 'Inayah). The sale of dog, cheetah etc. [i.e., the sale of cheetah is valid, and likewise the sale of elephant. (T, 'Ayni)] because they are animals that it is lawful to benefit from legally, so they are valuable property and subject to sale. Likewise, the sale of an elephant is valid because it is benefited from for carrying and riding. Regarding the sale of monkeys: two narrations from Abu Hanifa: in one, it is permissible because benefit can be taken from its skin; in the other, it is not permissible because it is for amusement, which is prohibited, and the correct is the first. And the sale of a cat is permissible because it is benefited from for repelling house pests and its skin. And it is permissible to sell every predator with fangs like lion, cheetah, hyena, wolf, and every bird with talons, due to the permissibility of benefiting from them legally, except the pig for it is impure in essence and it is not permissible to benefit from it, so likewise its sale is not permissible. And it is not permissible to sell land vermin like beetles, nor sea vermin like crabs and everything other than fish, even if not harmful, that has value like the skink. And the sale of snakes is permissible if they are used in medicine, otherwise not. In al-Ajnas: It is not proper for anyone to keep a dog in his house due to his saying: 'Whoever keeps a dog other than for hunting, two qirats are deducted from his reward every day,' except if he fears thieves or others, then there is no harm in keeping it. ('Ayni, Fath). And predators: from beasts of all types including the cat. (T, Miskin). And the dhimmi etc. [i.e., regarding the validity of his sale and purchase, the establishment of the three options, and the invalidity of his sale by what invalidates the sale of a Muslim. (Ma'adin)] due to his saying: 'Inform them that for them is what is for the Muslims and upon them is what is upon the Muslims,' and because they are responsible meaning in transactions by agreement, and in need of what sustains themselves like the Muslim. - And it is permissible to sell...
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage from the 'Chapter of Miscellaneous Matters' (Bab al-Mutafarriqat) of Kanz al-Daqa'iq consolidates several rulings that do not fit neatly into earlier chapters. The author begins with the permissibility of selling dogs, cheetahs, predators, birds, and dhimmis' transactions, then elaborates. The central ruling is that selling dogs is valid according to the Hanafi school, contrary to the Shafi'i and Hanbali positions. The proof is a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet ﷺ prohibited the sale of dogs except for hunting and herding dogs. The Hanafis argue that other hadiths prohibiting dog sales are abrogated or applied to the early period of Islam. They maintain that even untrained dogs provide benefit (guarding, barking), so they are valuable property. However, Abu Yusuf excludes vicious dogs incapable of training. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani adds the condition that the dog be trained or trainable. The passage then extends the ruling to other animals: cheetahs, elephants, lions, etc., as long as they are beneficial. Monkeys have two narrations from Abu Hanifa; the preferred view is permissibility due to benefit from their skin. Cats are permissible. Pigs are absolutely prohibited. Vermin and harmful insects are not salable. Snakes may be sold if used medicinally. The passage also addresses keeping dogs: it is discouraged due to the hadith about deduction of reward, but allowed for security. Finally, the passage addresses dhimmis: their transactions are treated like those of Muslims in all matters except wine and pigs, based on the prophetic instruction that dhimmis have the same rights and duties in worldly transactions. This lesson summarizes Hanafi fiqh on the sale of animals and the legal capacity of non-Muslim subjects.

Key Terms
الكلب العقور (al-kalb al-'aquur): A vicious dog that attacks and does not accept training.
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a covenant (dhimma) guaranteeing protection.
السحت (al-suht): Unlawful earnings or ill-gotten gains, often used for income from prohibited sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of dog is NOT valid to sell?
A) A trained hunting dog
B) A guard dog that barks at strangers
C) A vicious dog that cannot be trained
D) A dog used for herding livestock

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the sale of dogs and predatory animals provided they offer lawful benefit; a vicious dog that cannot be trained is not salable, and dhimmis have transactional capacity identical to Muslims except for wine and pork.

The Validity of Selling Dogs in Hanafi Fiqh
Inter-madhhab dispute on selling dogs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on selling dogs and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using textual evidence.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits selling dogs that provide benefit, distinguishing between trainable and untrainable dogs.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute over the sale of dogs. The Hanafi school, drawing on hadith and the concept of māl mutaqawwim, considers dogs valuable property when they serve a permissible purpose like hunting or guarding. The Shafi'i school maintains an absolute prohibition. Understanding each position's reasoning strengthens your ability to analyse ikhtilāf in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the matn declares the sale of dogs valid because they are māl mutaqawwim (valuable property), akin to a falcon used for hunting.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold the sale absolutely impermissible, citing the hadith that considers the price of a dog as suht (ill-gotten gain).
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis cite a hadith from Ibn Abbas that prohibits sale except for hunting or livestock dogs, and interpret the general prohibition as limited to early Islam when dogs were to be killed.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi condition
Shams al-A'imma requires the dog be trained or trainable; Abu Yusuf excludes untrainable ferocious dogs, which is considered the correct position (sahīḥ min al-madhhab).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a dog is destroyed, Hanafis require payment of its value, while Shafi'is do not, reflecting the difference in recognising dogs as valuable property.

صح بيع الكلب والفهد والسباع والطيور والذمي كالمسلم في بيع غير الخمر بيع الكلب لأنه مال متقوم آلة الاصطياد كالبازي، وعند الشافعي: لا يجوز بيعه أصلاً؛ لنهيه عليها عن الكلب، فقال: "إن من السحت مهر البغي وثمن الكلب" وبه قال أحمد، ولنا: ما روي عن ابن عباس لما أن النبي عليه هي عن بيع الكلب إلا كلب صيد أو ماشية، رواه الدار قطني. وفي رواية: "إلا كلب الضاري والمعلم"، وما رواه محمول على ابتداء الإسلام حين كان عليه أمر بقتل الكلاب، ولا فرق في ذلك بين جميع أنواع الكلاب المعلم وغير المعلم؛ لأن المعلم محل للبيع؛ لكونه منتفعاً به حقيقة وشرعا فيكون مالا، وأما غير المعلم؛ فلأنه ينتفع به بغير الاصطياد، فإن كل كلب يحفظ بيت صاحبه، ويمنع الأجانب عن الدخول في بيته، ويخبر عن الجائي بنباحه فساوى المعلم في الانتفاع به، وشرط شمس الأئمة لجواز أن يكون معلماً أو قابلاً للتعليم، وعن أبي يوسف: لا يصح بيع الكلب العقور الذي لا يقبل التعليم؛ لأنه لا ينتفع به، فصار كالهوام المؤذية، وهو الصحيح من المذهب، وإذا أتلف الرجل كلب الرجل يضمن عندنا قيمته خلافاً للشافعي. رمز الحقائق: ٨٠/٢] (فتح، عناية)
It is valid to sell a dog, a cheetah, predatory animals, birds, and a dhimmi is like a Muslim in selling other than wine... The sale of a dog is valid because it is valuable property, a tool for hunting like a falcon. According to al-Shafi'i: its sale is absolutely not permissible due to his prohibition regarding the dog, saying: 'Among bribery is the fee of the prostitute and the price of the dog.' Ahmad also said this. Our proof: what is narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet prohibited the sale of a dog except a hunting dog or a livestock dog', narrated by al-Daraqutni. And in another narration: 'except a predatory dog and a trained dog'. And what is narrated is interpreted as (applying to) the beginning of Islam when there was an order to kill dogs. There is no difference in this between all types of dogs, trained and untrained, because the trained dog is a subject of sale due to being beneficial in reality and legally, so it is wealth. As for the untrained, because it is benefited from other than hunting, for every dog guards its owner's house, prevents strangers from entering his house, and informs about the approaching through its barking, so it equals the trained in benefit. Shams al-A'imma stipulated for its permissibility that it be trained or capable of training. From Abu Yusuf: the sale of a ferocious dog that does not accept training is not valid because there is no benefit from it, so it becomes like harmful vermin, and this is the correct position of the school. If a man destroys another man's dog, he guarantees its value according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the ruling of selling dogs. The matn states that selling dogs is valid, and the commentary expands on the proof. The Hanafi position, as stated, is that dogs are valuable property because they provide benefit (hunting, guarding) both legally and in reality. This is contrasted with al-Shafi'i's absolute prohibition based on a hadith that considers the price of a dog as ill-gotten (suh). The Hanafi respond by citing another hadith from Ibn Abbas that permits sale of hunting and livestock dogs, and they interpret the prohibition as abrogated or limited to a time when dog killing was ordered. The commentary also distinguishes between trained and untrained dogs, noting that even untrained dogs provide guarding benefit. A precondition from Shams al-A'imma requires the dog be trained or trainable. Abu Yusuf adds that untrainable ferocious dogs cannot be sold because of no benefit, making them like harmful vermin. The passage concludes with a practical consequence: if someone destroys a dog, he must pay its value according to the Hanafis, unlike the Shafi'is.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and permissible to use; dogs meeting this criteria are valid for sale.
كلب عقور (kalb 'aqūr): A ferocious dog that does not accept training and from which no benefit is derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the sale of an untrained guard dog valid?
A) Because it is a tool for hunting like a falcon.
B) Because it benefits by guarding the house and barking at strangers.
C) Because the Prophet allowed the sale of all dogs.
D) Because it is a valuable commodity in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling dogs that provide a permissible benefit, with the condition that the dog is trained or trainable; untrainable ferocious dogs cannot be sold.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Ruling on Selling Predatory Animals and Birds
Series of rulings on selling predatory animals and birds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of various animals and identify which animals are sellable based on the principle of permissible benefit.

Core Idea
An animal is sellable if it provides permissible benefit; pigs and harmful vermin are excluded.

The Hanafi school provides clear guidelines on which animals may be sold. While many animals are considered valuable property due to their permissible use, some are inherently impure or harmful and cannot be transacted. This lesson examines the rulings for cheetahs, elephants, monkeys, cats, predators, and more, as well as the prohibition on selling pigs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Find the common rule that determines validity: the animal must be an object of permissible benefit (manfa'a mubaha).
See the commentary: 'because it is an animal from which benefit is legally permissible, so it is valuable property.'
2
List specific animals and rulings
Go through each animal mentioned (cheetah, elephant, monkey, cat, fanged predators, etc.) and note whether its sale is valid or invalid.
Pay attention to exceptions and conditions, e.g., snakes only valid if used for medicine.
3
Analyze the pig exception
Understand why the pig is uniquely excluded: it is najis al-ayn (intrinsically impure) and no permissible benefit exists.
This is a key distinction from other animals that may be impure but still beneficial.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on monkeys
Understand the two narrations from Abu Hanifa and why the first (permissibility) is considered correct.
The dispute hinges on whether amusement is a prohibited benefit affecting the validity of sale.
5
Consider the ruling on dogs
Read the hadith about keeping dogs and how it relates to sale (sale of dog is valid, but keeping as a pet is discouraged except for need).
The passage from 'al-Ajnas' adds practical guidance beyond sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيع الكلب والفهد إلخ: [ أي وصح بيع الفهد، وكذا بيع الفيل. (ط، عيني)] لأنها حيوان يجوز الانتفاع بها شرعاً، فيكون مالاً متقوماً، ومحل البيع، وكذا يصح بيع الفيل؛ لأنه ينتفع به حملاً وركوبا، وفي بيع القرد روايتان عن أبي حنيفة: في رواية: يجوز؛ لأنه يمكن الانتفاع بجلده، وفي رواية: لا يجوز؛ لأنه للتلهي وهو محظور، والصحيح هو الأول، وبيع الهرة جائز؛ لأنها ينتفع بها في دفع مؤذيات البيت وبجلدها، ويجوز بيع كل ذي ناب من السباع كالأسد والفهد والضبع والذئب، وذي مخلب من الطيور؛ لجواز الانتفاع بها شرعاً إلا الخنزير، فإنه نجس العين، ولا يجوز الانتفاع به، فكذا لا يجوز بيعه. ولا يجوز بيع هوام الأرض كالخنافس ولا هوام البحر كالسرطان وكل ما عدا السمك وإن لم تكن مؤذية ما له ثمن كالسقنقور ويجوز بيع الحيات إن انتفع بها في الأدوية، وإلا لا، وفي "الأجناس": لا ينبغي لأحد أن يتخذ كلباً في داره؛ لقوله عليه: "من اقتنى كلباً بغير صيد نقص من أجره كل يوم قيراطان" إلا أن يخاف من اللصوص أو من غيرهم فلا بأس بأن يتخذه. (عيني، فتح)
The sale of a dog, cheetah, etc.: [That is, the sale of a cheetah is valid, and likewise the sale of an elephant (T., 'Ayni)] because it is an animal from which benefit is legally permissible, so it is valuable property, and a subject of sale. Likewise the sale of an elephant is valid because benefit is derived from it for carrying and riding. Regarding the sale of a monkey, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: in one narration: it is valid because benefit can be derived from its skin; in another: it is not valid because it is for amusement, which is prohibited, and the correct is the first. The sale of a cat is valid because benefit is derived from it in repelling household pests and from its skin. The sale of every fanged predator like lion, cheetah, hyena, wolf, and every bird with claws is valid due to the permissibility of benefiting from them legally, except the pig, for it is ritually impure in substance, and benefit from it is not permissible, so its sale is likewise not permissible. The sale of land vermin like beetles, and sea vermin like crabs, and everything other than fish, even if not harmful, that has a price like the salamander, is not permissible. The sale of snakes is permissible if they are used for medicine, otherwise not. In 'al-Ajnas': It is not proper for anyone to keep a dog in his house because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever keeps a dog without hunting has two qirats deducted from his reward every day', unless he fears thieves or others, then there is no harm in keeping it. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings on selling various animals and birds. The general principle is that any animal from which legal benefit can be derived is valuable property and may be sold. Specific examples include: cheetahs (valid), elephants (valid for carrying/riding), monkeys (two views from Abu Hanifa, the correct being valid due to use of skin), cats (valid for pest control and skin), predators with fangs and birds with claws (valid for legal benefit). An exception is the pig, which is ritually impure and not beneficial, so sale is invalid. Also invalid are harmful vermin like beetles, crabs, and animals other than fish, even if they have a price. Snakes may be sold if used for medicine. The passage includes a note on keeping dogs: it is discouraged except for fear of thieves, citing a hadith about reward deduction.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Something that is inherently impure (swine), making it unsuitable for benefit and sale.
ذو ناب (dhū nāb): Animals possessing fangs (predators) that are permissible to sell because they can be benefited from (e.g., using their skins).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of a pig invalid?
A) Because it is harmful like vermin.
B) Because it is najis al-ayn and cannot be benefited from.
C) Because it is not a predatory animal.
D) Because it is not a bird with claws.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the validity of animal sales based on the presence of permissible benefit; pigs are excluded due to intrinsic impurity.

Ruling on Transactions of Dhimmis
Ruling on dhimmis' validity of sales and special treatment of wine and swine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi principle for dhimmi transactions and explain the exception for wine and swine with its proof from the practice of Umar (r.a.).

Core Idea
Dhimmis are like Muslims in the validity of sales, except for wine and swine which we allow because these are their property.

In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies the legal capacity of dhimmis in commercial transactions. While the general rule treats dhimmis as Muslims, a specific exception is made for wine and swine due to their religious significance. The proof is the directive of Umar ibn al-Khattab (r.a.) to his officials.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling for dhimmi transactions
The text states that dhimmis are like Muslims in the validity and nullity of sales. Determine the rule: their contracts are valid/invalid under the same conditions.
Based on hadith: 'They have what Muslims have, and upon them what is upon Muslims.'
2
Note the exception for wine and swine
Identify that wine and swine are treated differently: their sale by dhimmis is valid, unlike other prohibited items such as riba.
Reason: these are the most valuable wealth to them.
3
Examine the evidence from Umar's practice
Umar instructed officials not to take dead animals, wine, or swine as jizya, but to have the owners sell them and take the cash. This demonstrates permissibility.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi, considered valid proof.
4
Recognize that other prohibitions remain
The exception is narrow: only wine and swine are allowed. Riba, gambling, etc., remain invalid even for dhimmis.
This preserves the general rule that dhimmis are bound by Islamic transactional prohibitions.

Guarantor Adding Extra to Sale: Two Scenarios and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on third-party guarantee in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid third-party guarantees in a sale based on the phrasing of the guarantee, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A third party's guarantee is valid only if tied to the price (min al-thaman); otherwise it is a bribe (rishwa) and void.

This passage examines a scenario where a guarantor offers to pay an extra amount alongside a sale. The Hanafi school distinguishes between two phrasings: 'sawa al-alf' (additional to the thousand) makes the guarantee void, while 'min al-thaman' (from the price) makes it valid. The passage also notes a dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who reject any third-party addition. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid invalid contracts involving bribery.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two phrasings
Identify the two possible wordings used by the guarantor: 'sawa al-alf' (extra to the thousand) or 'min al-thaman' (from the price). These yield opposite rulings.
The key is whether the addition is explicitly tied to the price.
2
Apply the ruling to each phrase
Determine the hukm for each: 'sawa al-alf' renders the guarantee void (batil) because it is considered a bribe (rishwa); 'min al-thaman' renders it valid (sahih) and binding on the guarantor.
The sale remains valid in both cases; only the guarantee changes.
3
Trace the legal reasoning
Understand why 'min al-thaman' is valid: the text analogizes to khul', where a third party can pay the substitute. The condition is that the addition must be made in exchange for the sold item in form (sura) and naming (tasmiya). The phrase 'min al-thaman' fulfills this condition.
The Hanafi position uses qiyas to the permissibility of third-party payments in khul'.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Zufar (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i hold that any addition from a third party is invalid and does not attach to the contract. The Hanafi relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is valid if conditions are met.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different views on the scope of third-party stipulations.
5
Consider practical consequences
If the buyer ordered the guarantee, the guarantor can recover the amount from the buyer, and the addition becomes part of the price for profit calculation (murabahah) and preemption (shuf'ah).
Otherwise, the guarantor bears the payment without recourse to the buyer.

(عيني، فتح)
And if someone says: 'Your slave from Zaid for a thousand on condition that I am guarantor to you for a hundred extra the thousand', a person to another 'sell' for example, i.e., a thousand dirhams, i.e., a hundred dirhams, the price, so he sells, it is valid for a thousand and the guarantee is void. And if the extra is from the price, then the thousand is upon Zaid, and the hundred is upon the guarantor... So he sells the man his slave with this condition, it is valid for a thousand and the guarantee of the hundred is void from the guarantor; because the extra is not from the price, but rather it is a commitment of money initially, and it is a bribe, which is forbidden, so the guarantee is not valid. So he takes from the buyer a thousand and nothing is upon the guarantor. ('Ayni, 'Inayah) With a thousand dirhams and he is not... your slave this, sell the thousand, and the guarantee is void: i.e., the seller takes the thousand from the buyer and nothing upon the guarantor (T). And if the extra is from the price: i.e., this speaker in the mentioned case says: 'Sell your slave from this man, for example, for a thousand dirhams on condition that I am guarantor to you for a hundred from the price', then the sale is valid, and the thousand is incumbent upon the buyer, and the hundred is incumbent upon the guarantor; because the addition is permissible as from the buyer even if nothing is received in return for it, it is permissible from a third party if nothing is received in return for the substitute. Thus it becomes like the substitute in khul' where stipulating it upon a third party is permissible as it is permissible upon the woman, since nothing is received in return for the substitute from either. However, the condition for the validity of the addition is that it be made in exchange for the sold item in form and naming. So if the person says 'min' 'al-thaman', its condition is fulfilled and it is valid; if he does not say 'min' 'al-thaman', its condition is not fulfilled, so it is not valid. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: the addition is not valid at all, and it is not binding upon him. This is based on that the addition is not valid and does not attach to the original contract according to them, but it is valid according to us. So if the addition from a third party is valid, he does not collect it from the buyer, and it does not appear in the seller's right to retain the sold item, nor in the case of profit or preemption, except if it was with the buyer's order, then it appears in all cases so that the guarantor recovers it from the buyer and calculates profit on it and on the thousand, and the seller does not demand the addition from the buyer even if it was with his order. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a complex legal scenario involving a third-party guarantor who offers an additional sum in a sale. The text distinguishes two cases: if the guarantor says 'sawa al-alf' (extra to the thousand), the guarantee is void because it is a bribe (rishwa). If he says 'min al-thaman' (from the price), the guarantee is valid and the guarantor must pay the extra amount. The reason is that in the first case, the addition is not part of the price but a gratuitous payment, which is impermissible. In the second case, the addition is tied to the price, making it a valid stipulation. The passage also notes a disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who deny the validity of any addition from a third party. The Hanafi position allows it if properly worded. Furthermore, if the buyer requested the guarantee, the guarantor can recover the amount from the buyer, and it becomes part of the price for profit calculation and preemption. The condition for validity is the phrase 'min al-thaman'.

Key Terms
رشوة (rishwa): A bribe; the extra payment not tied to the price is considered a bribe and is prohibited, voiding the guarantee.
زيادة من الثمن (ziyādat min al-thaman): An addition to the price, phrased as 'from the price', which makes the guarantee valid; it is treated as part of the consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The sale is valid and the guarantor must pay the hundred.
C) The sale is valid but the guarantee is void.
D) The guarantee is valid only if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a third-party guarantee in a sale hinges on whether the extra amount is explicitly tied to the price; otherwise it is a forbidden bribe.

Selling Pigs and Carcasses to Dhimmis and the Prohibition of Bribes
Ruling with proof on selling forbidden items to dhimmis and bribe

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling pigs and carcasses to dhimmis and explain why an extra payment separate from the price is void as a bribe.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit selling pigs and carcasses to dhimmis; any payment beyond the agreed price is a bribe and void.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses two distinct but connected rulings: the permissibility of transacting in pigs and carcasses with non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis), and the invalidity of extra payments that function as bribes in a sale. The first part is a direct statement of Hanafi fiqh, while the second part illustrates how a guarantee for an additional sum beyond the price is deemed a prohibited bribe, rendering that guarantee void. Together, they clarify the boundaries of permissible trade with dhimmis and the strict prohibition of corrupt inducements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on pigs and carcasses
The matn states 'And the pig and the carcass that did not die of natural causes.' Determine the legal status of selling these items to dhimmis according to Hanafi school.
The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) because these items have value for dhimmis, even though they are najis for Muslims.
2
Analyze the bribe scenario
The commentary describes a case where a third party guarantees the seller an extra 100 dirhams beyond the 1000 price. Identify the key condition: this extra amount is separate from the sale price.
The extra 100 is not part of the price; it is offered to induce the seller to sell.
3
Apply the principle: bribe is haram
The text states that such an extra payment is a bribe (rishwah), which is explicitly forbidden in Islamic law. Therefore, the guarantee of that payment is void.
The sale itself remains valid for the base price of 1000, because the bribe is an independent corrupt element.
4
Conclude the combined ruling
Combine the two rulings: selling forbidden items to dhimmis is permissible, but any corrupt inducement like a bribe invalidates that specific arrangement.
This showcases the Hanafi principle that the presence of a haram element does not necessarily void the entire contract if separable.
