Third-Party Guarantee with Increase from the Price
Ruling on validity of guarantee when increase is part of the price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid third-party guarantee of an increase from the price and explain the Hanafi position against the view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A third-party increase is valid only if explicitly stated as 'from the price'.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in sales (buyu) concerning guarantees (kafalah). When a third party offers to guarantee a portion of the price, the wording matters critically. The Hanafi school upholds its validity under a specific condition, analogizing it to the compensation in khul' divorce, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i reject it entirely. Understanding this dispute illuminates Hanafi reasoning and the importance of contractual precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? A third party (outsider) guarantees an increase from the price in a sale. The Hanafi position: valid if the guarantor says 'min al-thaman' (from the price).
The underlying act is the sale contract with a third-party guarantee attached.
2
State the condition for validity
The condition is that the increase must be explicitly linked to the price by using the phrase 'from the price'. Without it, the increase is invalid (like a bribe).
This condition is derived from the text: 'but one condition... that it be matched with the sold item in form and naming.'
3
Follow the opposing view and its proof
Zufar (within Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i hold that such an increase is invalid entirely. Their reasoning: the increase does not attach to the original contract and is separate, thus void.
Zufar is considered an opposing voice within the Hanafi school, making this an intra-Hanafi dispute.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds with an analogy (qiyas) to khul' compensation: just as an outsider can pay the khul' compensation to the husband even though nothing is given in exchange, so can an outsider guarantee an increase from the price. In both, the payer receives nothing directly in return.
This analogy addresses the concern that the increase is a gratuitous payment without consideration.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Hanafi view, the guarantor is liable for the increase and the seller cannot demand it from the buyer. Under the opposing view, the guarantor is not liable at all.
This distinction matters for who bears the financial burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن زاد إلخ: بأن قال هذا القائل في الصورة المذكورة: بع عبدك من هذا الرجل مثلاً بألف درهم على أني ضامن لك مائة من الثمن سوى صح البيع، والألف يجب على المشتري، والمائة تجب على الضامن؛ لأن الزيادة كما تجوز من المشتري وإن لم يسلم له بمقابلتها شيء تجوز الأج من جنبي إذا لم يسلم له شيء بمقابلة البدل، فصار كبدل الخلع حيث يجوز اشتراطه على الأجنبي كما يجوز اشتراطه على المرأة؛ إذ لا يسلم لهما شيء مقابلة ،البدل، لكن من شرط صحة الزيادة أن تقابل بالمبيع صورة وتسمية. فإذا قال الرجل: "من" "الثمن" فقد وجد شرطها فتصح وإذا لم يقل: "من" "الثمن" لم يوجد شرطها فلا تصح، وعند زفر والشافعي: لا يجوز الزيادة أصلاً، ولا يلزمه، وهذا مبني على أن الزيادة لا تصح ولا تلتحق بأصل العقد عندهما وتصح عندنا، فإذا جازت الزيادة من الأجنبي لا يرجع بها على المشتري، ولا تظهر في حق حبس البائع المبيع والمرابحة والشفعة إلا إذا كانت بأمر المشتري، فإنها تظهر في حق الكل حتى يرجع الضامن بها على المشتري ويرابح عليها وعلى الألف، ولا يطالب البائع المشتري بالزيادة وإن كانت بأمره. (عيني، فتح)
And if he increased from the price etc.: That is, this speaker in the mentioned scenario says: Sell your slave to this man for example for a thousand dirhams on condition that I guarantee you one hundred from the price besides, the sale is valid, and the thousand is obligatory upon the buyer, and the hundred is obligatory upon the guarantor; because the increase is permissible from the buyer even if nothing is given to him in exchange, it is permissible from the outsider even if nothing is given to him in exchange, so it becomes like the compensation in khul' where stipulating it upon an outsider is permissible just as it is permissible upon the woman; since nothing is given to either in exchange for the compensation. But one condition for validity of the increase is that it be matched with the sold item in form and naming. So when the man says 'from the price', the condition is found and it is valid, and if he does not say 'from the price', the condition is not found and it is not valid. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, the increase is not valid at all and does not oblige him. This is based on the fact that the increase is not valid and does not attach to the original contract according to them, and it is valid according to us. So when the increase is valid from the outsider, he does not seek recourse from the buyer for it, and it does not appear in the right of the seller to detain the sold item, nor in markup or pre-emption, except if it was by the buyer's order, then it appears for all rights so the guarantor can recourse to the buyer and markup on both the increase and the thousand, and the seller does not demand the buyer for the increase even if it was by his order. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a third party guarantees an amount that is part of the price. If the guarantor says 'I guarantee you 100 from the price', the sale is valid and the guarantor is liable for the 100. The condition is that the increase must be explicitly stated as part of the price. Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree, holding that any such increase is invalid. The Hanafi position is preferred, with the condition of proper wording.

Key Terms
بدل الخلع (badal al-khul'): Compensation given by the wife or a third party to the husband for divorce by khul'.
الزيادة (al-ziyādah): An extra amount over the agreed price, either from the buyer or a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a third party's increase to be valid according to the Hanafis?
A) The increase must equal half the price.
B) The increase must be given after the sale.
C) The guarantor must say 'from the price'.
D) The buyer must agree.

Key Takeaway
A third-party guarantee of an increase from the price is valid only if explicitly stated as 'from the price', analogized to khul' compensation, and the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Possession by Consummation
Ruling on possession through marriage and intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a marriage contract alone does not constitute possession (qabd) of a purchased slave-girl in Hanafi fiqh, and identify the specific act that does.

Core Idea
Intercourse by the husband counts as the buyer's possession; the marriage contract alone does not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the boundary between ownership and possession. When a buyer purchases a slave-girl and marries her off before taking physical possession, the marriage is valid, but does it make the buyer a possessor? The Hanafi ruling draws a fine distinction between acts that merely affect legal status and those that actually diminish value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
State: a buyer purchases a slave-girl, then marries her to another man before taking physical possession. The marriage is valid because the buyer has complete ownership of the neck (milk al-raqaba). However, the question is whether the marriage contract itself constitutes possession (qabd) of the slave-girl.
The passage says marriage is valid 'bikhilaf al-bay' (unlike sale) where selling before possession is not permitted.
2
Analyze the analogy and its rejection
By analogy (qiyas), one might argue that the marriage contract is a notional defect (ta'yib hukmi) and thus possession, similar to tadbir (declaration of freedom after death) or emancipation. However, the Hanafi school rejects this analogy because the marriage contract does not actually diminish the slave-girl's value; the buyer can still sell her. Only acts that destroy wealth (like tadbir or emancipation) or cause real deficiency (like intercourse) count as possession.
The passage explicitly distinguishes: 'bikhilaf al-tadbir wa al-i'taq' – unlike tadbir and emancipation.
3
Identify the act that constitutes possession
If the husband has intercourse with the slave-girl, that act is attributed to the buyer and counts as possession. The buyer becomes a qabid because the intercourse occurred with his permission and it causes a real physical change. The mere marriage contract (without consummation) does not suffice.
The passage says: 'in wati'aha al-zawj kana al-mushtari qabidan... wa la huwa 'aqdahu' – if the husband has intercourse, the buyer is possessor, not his contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووطء زوج المشتراة قبض لا عقده، ومن اشترى عبداً فغاب، أي المشتري قبل القبض وقبل أداء الثمن. (عيني) فبرهن البائع على بيعه، وغيبته معروفة، لم يُبع لدين البائع، وإلا بيع لدينه، ولو غاب العبد لا يبيعه القاضي باعه القاضي لدين البائع ووطء زوج إلخ صورته: اشترى أمة، فزوجها المشتري قبل القبض، صح النكاح؛ لوجود الملك في الرقبة على الكمال، بخلاف البيع حيث لا يجوز، فإذا جاز النكاح، فإن وطئها الزوج كان المشتري قابضاً للأمة؛ لأن الوطء من الزوج إنما حصل بتسليط المشتري، فصار منسوباً إليه كأنه فعله بنفسه لا عقده: أي وإن لم يطأها الزوج بعد النكاح فليس مجرد عقد زوج الأمة المشتراة بقبض؛ لأنه لم يتصل بها من المشتري فعل يوجب النقصان في الذات، وإن كان القياس أن يكون قبضاً بنفس التزويج؛ لأنه تعييب حكمي، بخلاف التدبير والإعتاق؛ لأن المالية قد تلفت به بثبوت حقيقة الحرية أو حقه، ومن ضرورته يصير قابضاً [ رمز الحقائق: ٨٢/٢] (فتح) عبداً: أي منقولا؛ إذ العقار لا يبيعه القاضي. (ط) فغاب أي المشتري قبل القبض وقبل أداء الثمن. (عيني) على بيعه: أي على أنه باع هذا العبد من فلان وأنه غاب قبل أن ينقد الثمن. (عيني) وغيبته معروفة: فطلب البائع من أن القاضي يبيعه لدينه. (عيني) لم يبع لدين البائع أي إذا اشترى عبداً، فغاب المشتري قبل قبضه وأداء ثمنه، وأقام البائع البينة على ذلك حال كون المشتري غائباً غيبة معروفة، وطلب المشتري من القاضي بيع العبد في دينه لم يبع القاضي العبد في دين البائع؛ لأن البائع يصل إلى حقه بالذهاب إلى المشتري، فلا حاجة إلى العبد؛ لأن فيه إبطال حق المشتري في العبد. وإلا بيع لدينه: أي وإن لم تكن غيبة معروفة يعني لا يدرى مكانه باع القاضي العبد لدين البائع؛ لأن القاضي نصب ناظراً للعاجزين الذين عجزوا عن النظر لأنفسهم، والحاجة إلى النظر ماسة لهما جميعاً، أما المشتري: فلأن العبد ملكه، وهو محتاج إلى النفقة، فيتخلص منها، ويبرأ ذمته من دين البائع وأما البائع: فيصل به إلى حقه، ويتخلص عن سقوط حقه في الثمن إذا هلك المبيع في يده. فإن قلت القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؟ قلنا: ليست بينة البائع ههنا للقضاء على الغائب، وإنما هي لنفي التهمة وانكشاف الحال فإن قلت هذا البيع قبل القبض وهو غير جائز فكيف يباع؟ قلت: ليس هذا البيع بمقصود هنا؛ لأن المقصود إحياء حقه، وفي ضمنه يصح بيعه، والشيء قد يصح ضمنا وإن لم يصح قصدا، ثم إذا باع القاضي العبد وأعطى ثمنه للبائع، فإن فضل شيء يمسكه للمشتري الغائب؛ لأنه بدل ملكه، وإن لم يف ثمنه الثاني بالدين وبقي شيء من الدين يتبع البائع المشتري إذا ظفر به. (عيني، فتح)
And the intercourse of the husband of the purchased female slave is possession, not her marriage contract. And whoever bought a slave and then the buyer disappeared, i.e., before possession and before payment of the price. So the seller established proof of his sale and his absence is known: the slave is not sold for the seller's debt; otherwise it is sold for his debt. And if the slave disappears, the judge does not sell it; the judge sells it for the seller's debt. And intercourse of the husband etc. Its form: he bought a slave-girl and then the buyer married her off before possession: the marriage is valid due to complete ownership of the neck, unlike sale where it is not permitted. So when the marriage is valid, if the husband has intercourse, the buyer is considered possessor of the slave-girl because the intercourse by the husband occurred by the buyer's permission, so it is attributed to the buyer as if he did it himself, not his contract. That is, if the husband did not have intercourse after marriage, the mere marriage contract of the purchased slave-girl is not possession because no act causing deficiency in the substance is connected from the buyer, even though by analogy it would be possession by the mere marriage because it is a notional defect, unlike tadbir and emancipation because the wealth has been destroyed by the establishment of true freedom or its right, and by its necessity he becomes possessor. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 82/2] (Fath) Slave: i.e., movable property, since real estate is not sold by the judge. (T) So he disappeared: i.e., the buyer before possession and before payment of price. (Ayni) On his sale: i.e., that he sold this slave to so-and-so and that he disappeared before paying the price. (Ayni) And his absence is known: so the seller asks the judge to sell the slave for his debt. (Ayni) It is not sold for the seller's debt: i.e., when he bought a slave and the buyer disappeared before possession and payment of price, and the seller established evidence of that while the buyer is absent with known absence, and the seller asks the judge to sell the slave for his debt: the judge does not sell the slave for the seller's debt because the seller can reach his right by going to the buyer, so there is no need for the slave, because that would void the buyer's right in the slave. Otherwise it is sold for his debt: i.e., if the absence is not known, meaning his location is unknown, the judge sells the slave for the seller's debt because the judge is appointed as supervisor for those unable to look after themselves, and the need for supervision is pressing for both: as for the buyer, because the slave is his property and he needs maintenance, so he gets rid of it and his liability is cleared from the seller's debt; and as for the seller, he reaches his right and avoids losing his right to the price if the sold item perishes in his hand. If you say: judgment against an absent person is not permissible? We say: the seller's evidence here is not for judgment against the absent person, but rather to remove suspicion and uncover the situation. If you say: this sale before possession is not permissible, so how is it sold? We say: this sale is not intended here; rather the intention is to revive his right, and within that the sale becomes valid, and a thing may be valid implicitly even if not valid explicitly. Then when the judge sells the slave and gives its price to the seller, if anything remains, he keeps it for the absent buyer because it is the substitute for his property; and if the second price does not cover the debt and some debt remains, the seller pursues the buyer when he finds him. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, if a buyer purchases a slave-girl and marries her off before taking possession, the marriage is valid but does not constitute possession. However, if the husband has intercourse with her, that act counts as possession by the buyer because it is done with his permission. Second, if a buyer disappears after purchasing a slave but before taking possession and paying the price, the seller can ask the judge to sell the slave. If the buyer's absence is known, the judge does not sell because the seller can go to him. If the absence is unknown, the judge sells to protect both parties' interests. This sale is valid by necessity.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical or legal control of the purchased item. Acts like intercourse or emancipation can constitute constructive possession.
تعديب (ta'dhib): Emancipation by tadbir (declaration that slave is free after master's death). It is considered a notional defect and hence possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following constitutes possession (qabd) of a purchased slave-girl?
A) The marriage contract alone.
B) The husband's intercourse.
C) The buyer's intention to possess.
D) The price payment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer of a slave-girl becomes a possessor only when an act that diminishes her value occurs, such as the husband's intercourse, not by the mere marriage contract.

Sale of Absent Buyer's Slave by Judge
Ruling on sale of absent buyer's slave by judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge may sell a slave purchased by an absent buyer before possession and payment, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
The judge sells the absent buyer's slave only if the buyer's whereabouts are unknown; if known, the seller must seek him directly.

This passage addresses a practical problem: a buyer disappears after purchasing a slave but before taking possession and paying the price. The judge's intervention balances the rights of seller and buyer. The ruling depends on whether the buyer's absence is known or unknown.

Guided Path
1
Describe the scenario
A buyer purchases a slave, but before taking possession and paying the price, he disappears. The seller comes to the judge with proof of the sale and the buyer's absence.
The passage specifies 'before possession and before payment'.
2
Determine if the buyer's absence is known or unknown
If the buyer's location is known (ghayba ma'rufa), the judge does not sell the slave because the seller can go to the buyer to claim his right. Selling would void the buyer's property right unnecessarily.
The passage says 'la yuba' li dayn al-ba'i' – not sold for the seller's debt.
3
Apply the alternative ruling
If the buyer's location is unknown (ghayba ghayr ma'rufa), the judge sells the slave to satisfy the seller's debt. The judge acts as a guardian for both parties: the buyer is freed from maintenance expenses, and the seller receives his price.
The passage says 'illa buy'a li daynih' – otherwise it is sold for his debt.
4
Address the objections
Objection 1: Sale without possession is normally invalid. Answer: This sale is by necessity (darura) and valid by implication. Objection 2: Judgment against an absent person is not allowed. Answer: The evidence is not for judgment but to clarify the situation.
The passage responds: 'laysa hadha al-bay' bi-maqsud... wa al-shay' qad yasihhu damnan' – this sale is not intended, but it is valid by implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووطء زوج المشتراة قبض لا عقده، ومن اشترى عبداً فغاب، أي المشتري قبل القبض وقبل أداء الثمن. (عيني) لم يبع لدين البائع أي إذا اشترى عبداً، فغاب المشتري قبل قبضه وأداء ثمنه، وأقام البائع البينة على ذلك حال كون المشتري غائباً غيبة معروفة، وطلب المشتري من القاضي بيع العبد في دينه لم يبع القاضي العبد في دين البائع؛ لأن البائع يصل إلى حقه بالذهاب إلى المشتري، فلا حاجة إلى العبد؛ لأن فيه إبطال حق المشتري في العبد. وإلا بيع لدينه: أي وإن لم تكن غيبة معروفة يعني لا يدرى مكانه باع القاضي العبد لدين البائع؛ لأن القاضي نصب ناظراً للعاجزين الذين عجزوا عن النظر لأنفسهم، والحاجة إلى النظر ماسة لهما جميعاً، أما المشتري: فلأن العبد ملكه، وهو محتاج إلى النفقة، فيتخلص منها، ويبرأ ذمته من دين البائع وأما البائع: فيصل به إلى حقه، ويتخلص عن سقوط حقه في الثمن إذا هلك المبيع في يده. فإن قلت القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؟ قلنا: ليست بينة البائع ههنا للقضاء على الغائب، وإنما هي لنفي التهمة وانكشاف الحال فإن قلت هذا البيع قبل القبض وهو غير جائز فكيف يباع؟ قلت: ليس هذا البيع بمقصود هنا؛ لأن المقصود إحياء حقه، وفي ضمنه يصح بيعه، والشيء قد يصح ضمنا وإن لم يصح قصدا، ثم إذا باع القاضي العبد وأعطى ثمنه للبائع، فإن فضل شيء يمسكه للمشتري الغائب؛ لأنه بدل ملكه، وإن لم يف ثمنه الثاني بالدين وبقي شيء من الدين يتبع البائع المشتري إذا ظفر به. (عيني، فتح)

Commentary

This sale is valid by necessity.

Key Terms
غيبة معروفة (ghayba ma'rufa): Known absence: the location of the absent person is known. In this case, the judge does not sell the slave because the seller can go to him.
غيبة غير معروفة (ghayba ghayr ma'rufa): Unknown absence: the location of the absent person is unknown. The judge may sell to protect rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the judge sell the slave of an absent buyer?
A) Always, to protect the seller's right.
B) Only if the buyer's absence is unknown.
C) Only if the buyer has paid part of the price.
D) Only if the slave is movable property.

Key Takeaway
The judge may sell an absent buyer's slave before possession only when the buyer's whereabouts are unknown, to balance the rights of both parties.

Consummation as Possession: The Husband's Act and Buyer's Possession
Ruling on possession through authorized act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why consummation by the husband of a purchased slave-girl constitutes possession by the buyer and distinguish it from the marriage contract alone.

Core Idea
Possession requires a physical act; the marriage contract alone is insufficient.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a nuanced issue in sales: when does a buyer take possession of a purchased slave-girl who has been married off? The author rules that consummation by the husband, authorized by the buyer, counts as the buyer's possession. This ruling highlights the Hanafi emphasis on tangible acts over mere legal formalities for establishing possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of the husband cohabiting with the purchased slave-girl. The ruling is that this act constitutes possession (qabd) by the buyer.
Possession is a key concept in sales, transferring risk and ownership effects.
2
Locate the condition for possession
The ruling depends on the buyer's authorization. The buyer married the slave-girl to the husband, thereby granting him permission to cohabit. This authorization makes the husband's act attributable to the buyer.
This is similar to agency; the buyer's consent makes the husband's act like the buyer's own.
3
Contrast with the marriage contract alone
If the husband does not consummate, the marriage contract by itself does not constitute possession. Why? Because no physical act causing a reduction in the slave-girl's value has been performed by the buyer.
The text explicitly excludes tadbir and emancipation, which do affect value and count as possession.
4
Understand the distinction from other acts
The author distinguishes consummation from tadbir (promise of emancipation after death) and immediate emancipation. Those acts directly affect the slave's legal status and monetary value, so they count as possession. Consummation does not cause a permanent loss of value but is still a physical act.
This shows that possession can be constructive through authorized acts.
5
Apply the principle to a hypothetical scenario
Imagine a buyer purchases a car and authorizes a friend to take delivery. According to this principle, the friend's physical taking of the car would count as the buyer's possession. Similarly, if the buyer authorizes someone to test-drive it, that may also be possession.
Modern analogy: think of authorized delivery or use as constructive possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووطء زوج المشتراة قبض لا عقده، ومن اشترى عبداً فغاب، أي أقام البينة الواو للحال لمشتريها أي فبرهن البائع على بيعه، وغيبته معروفة، لم يُبع لدين البائع، وإلا بيع لدينه، ولو غاب العبد لا يبيعه القاضي باعه القاضي لدين البائع ووطء زوج إلخ صورته: اشترى أمة، فزوجها المشتري قبل القبض، صح النكاح؛ لوجود الملك في الرقبة على الكمال، بخلاف البيع حيث لا يجوز، فإذا جاز النكاح، فإن وطئها الزوج كان المشتري قابضاً للأمة؛ لأن الوطء من الزوج إنما حصل بتسليط المشتري، فصار منسوباً إليه كأنه فعله بنفسه لا عقده: أي وإن لم يطأها الزوج بعد النكاح فليس مجرد عقد زوج الأمة المشتراة بقبض؛ لأنه لم يتصل بها من المشتري فعل يوجب النقصان في الذات، وإن كان القياس أن يكون قبضاً بنفس التزويج؛ لأنه تعييب حكمي، بخلاف التدبير والإعتاق؛ لأن المالية قد تلفت به بثبوت حقيقة الحرية أو حقه، ومن ضرورته يصير قابضاً [ رمز الحقائق: ٨٢/٢] (فتح)
And the coitus of the husband of the purchased (slave-girl) is possession, not the contract of marriage. And whoever bought a slave and the [buyer] disappeared... the picture: He bought a slave-girl, then married her before possession, the marriage is valid due to complete ownership of the essence, unlike sale where it is not permissible. When the marriage is valid, if the husband cohabits, the buyer becomes a possessor of the slave-girl because the coitus by the husband only occurred by the buyer's authorization, so it is attributed to him as if he did it himself. Not his contract: i.e., if the husband did not cohabit after the marriage, then merely the contract of marriage of the purchased slave-girl is not possession because no act causing a reduction in the essence has been performed by the buyer, even though by analogy marriage itself would be possession because it is a legal defect, unlike tadbir and emancipation because the monetary value is destroyed by the establishment of the reality of freedom or its right, and by necessity he becomes a possessor.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation on the concept of possession (qabd) of a purchased slave-girl. The author states that coitus by the husband of a purchased slave-girl constitutes possession by the buyer, not merely the marriage contract. The reasoning is that the buyer authorized the marriage and thereby implicitly allowed the husband to cohabit, so the buyer is deemed to have taken possession through the husband's act. However, if no coitus occurs, the marriage contract alone does not count as possession because there is no physical act by the buyer that reduces the value of the slave-girl. This is distinguished from tadbir (promise of emancipation after death) and immediate emancipation, which do constitute possession because they directly affect the slave's legal status and monetary value. The passage follows the Hanafi position that possession requires a tangible act, not merely a legal disposition.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical control of the purchased item, transferring risk and ownership effects.
تزويج (tazwij): The marriage contract; the act of giving a slave-girl in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the marriage of a purchased slave-girl constitute possession by the buyer?
A) As soon as the marriage contract is made.
B) Only if the husband consummates the marriage.
C) Only if the buyer himself cohabits with her.
D) Never, because marriage does not affect possession.

Key Takeaway
Possession by the buyer is established through a physical act (consummation) authorized by the buyer, not through the marriage contract alone.

Judicial Sale of an Absent Buyer's Slave for Debt
Ruling on whether the judge can sell the slave when the buyer disappears before paying and taking possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios for judicial sale of an absent buyer's property and explain the legal reasoning (illa) that distinguishes them.

Core Idea
The judge may sell the absent buyer's property only when his location is unknown – necessity overrides the rule against selling before possession.

In this passage, we examine a specific case where a buyer disappears after purchasing a slave but before paying and taking possession. The seller asks the judge to sell the slave to recover the debt. The ruling distinguishes based on whether the buyer's whereabouts are known or unknown. This lesson will walk through the two scenarios and the rational arguments (qiyas) behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and the relief sought
A buyer purchases a slave, disappears before paying and before taking possession. The seller asks the judge to sell the slave to satisfy the debt.
The slave is movable property; real estate is not sold by the judge in this context.
2
Distinguish the two cases: known vs unknown absence
If the buyer's whereabouts are known, the judge does not sell the slave; the seller must pursue the buyer directly. If the buyer's location is unknown, the judge sells the slave.
The key variable is whether the seller can reach the buyer on their own.
3
Analyze the illa (effective cause) for the sale
When the buyer is untraceable, the seller cannot access his right otherwise. The judge, as supervisor for the incapable, intervenes to protect both parties: the buyer is freed from maintenance liability and the seller recovers his debt.
The illa is the inability to resolve the debt through direct pursuit.
4
Examine objections and Hanafi responses
Objections: (1) Judgment against an absent person is not allowed. (2) Sale before possession is invalid. Responses: (1) The evidence is for clarification, not for a binding judgment. (2) The sale is valid collaterally, not primarily – it is a means to revive the seller's right.
Collateral validity (صح ضمنا) is a Hanafi principle used to avoid harm.
5
Determine the practical outcome
The judge sells the slave, gives the price to the seller. Any surplus is kept for the absent buyer. If the price is insufficient, the seller may pursue the buyer later.
The sale discharges the buyer's debt and relieves maintenance obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبداً: أي منقولا؛ إذ العقار لا يبيعه القاضي. (ط) فغاب أي المشتري قبل القبض وقبل أداء الثمن. (عيني) على بيعه: أي على أنه باع هذا العبد من فلان وأنه غاب قبل أن ينقد الثمن. (عيني) وغيبته معروفة: فطلب البائع من أن القاضي يبيعه لدينه. (عيني) لم يبع لدين البائع أي إذا اشترى عبداً، فغاب المشتري قبل قبضه وأداء ثمنه، وأقام البائع البينة على ذلك حال كون المشتري غائباً غيبة معروفة، وطلب المشتري من القاضي بيع العبد في دينه لم يبع القاضي العبد في دين البائع؛ لأن البائع يصل إلى حقه بالذهاب إلى المشتري، فلا حاجة إلى العبد؛ لأن فيه إبطال حق المشتري في العبد. وإلا بيع لدينه: أي وإن لم تكن غيبة معروفة يعني لا يدرى مكانه باع القاضي العبد لدين البائع؛ لأن القاضي نصب ناظراً للعاجزين الذين عجزوا عن النظر لأنفسهم، والحاجة إلى النظر ماسة لهما جميعاً، أما المشتري: فلأن العبد ملكه، وهو محتاج إلى النفقة، فيتخلص منها، ويبرأ ذمته من دين البائع وأما البائع: فيصل به إلى حقه، ويتخلص عن سقوط حقه في الثمن إذا هلك المبيع في يده. بيع فإن قلت القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؟ قلنا: ليست بينة البائع ههنا للقضاء على الغائب، وإنما هي لنفي التهمة وانكشاف الحال فإن قلت هذا البيع قبل القبض وهو غير جائز فكيف يباع؟ قلت: ليس هذا البيع بمقصود هنا؛ لأن المقصود إحياء حقه، وفي ضمنه يصح بيعه، والشيء قد يصح ضمنا وإن لم يصح قصدا، ثم إذا باع القاضي العبد وأعطى ثمنه للبائع، فإن فضل شيء يمسكه للمشتري الغائب؛ لأنه بدل ملكه، وإن لم يف ثمنه الثاني بالدين وبقي شيء من الدين يتبع البائع المشتري إذا ظفر به. (عيني، فتح)
A slave: i.e., movable property because the judge does not sell real estate. (T) So he disappeared: i.e., the buyer before possession and before payment of the price. (Ayni) Upon his sale: i.e., that he sold this slave from so-and-so and that he disappeared before paying the price. (Ayni) And his absence is known: so the seller asks the judge to sell it for his debt. (Ayni) It is not sold for the seller's debt: i.e., if he bought a slave and the buyer disappeared before possessing and paying the price, and the seller provides evidence of that while the buyer is absent with known whereabouts, and the seller asks the judge to sell the slave for his debt, the judge does not sell the slave for the seller's debt because the seller can reach his right by going to the buyer, so there is no need for the slave, and because it would invalidate the buyer's right in the slave. Otherwise it is sold for his debt: i.e., if the absence is unknown, meaning his location is not known, the judge sells the slave for the seller's debt because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for those unable to look after themselves, and the need for supervision is pressing for both. As for the buyer: the slave is his property and he needs maintenance, so he is relieved of that and his liability for the seller's debt is discharged. As for the seller: he reaches his right and avoids losing his right to the price if the sold item perishes in his hand. If it is said: judgment against an absent person is not permissible; we say: the seller's evidence here is not for judgment against the absent person, but rather to remove suspicion and clarify the situation. If it is said: this sale is before possession and it is not permissible, so how is it sold? We say: this sale is not intended here, because the objective is to revive his right, and in its wake the sale becomes valid; a thing may be valid collaterally even if not valid primarily. Then when the judge sells the slave and gives its price to the seller, if something remains he keeps it for the absent buyer because it is the substitute for his property; if the price does not cover the debt and something of the debt remains, the seller pursues the buyer if he finds him.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the permissibility of a judge selling a slave bought by an absent buyer who has not paid the price. The ruling distinguishes two scenarios: (1) if the buyer's whereabouts are known, the judge does not sell the slave; the seller must seek the buyer directly. (2) If the buyer's location is unknown, the judge sells the slave to satisfy the seller's debt. The reasoning is based on the judge's role as supervisor for those unable to act for themselves. The passage also addresses potential objections: that judgment against an absent person is not allowed, and that selling before possession (qabd) is generally prohibited. The response is that the evidence is not to establish a judgment but to clarify the situation, and the sale is valid collaterally to revive the seller's right, even though it would not be valid as a primary transaction. Any surplus after debt is kept for the absent buyer.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical control of the purchased item.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): A judicial decision against an absent person, generally not allowed in Hanafi law except in certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the buyer is absent but his location is known.
B) When the buyer's location is unknown.
C) Whenever the seller requests it.
D) Only if the buyer has given permission.

Key Takeaway
The judge may sell the absent buyer's property for the seller's debt only when the buyer's location is unknown; otherwise the seller must pursue the buyer directly.

The Present Buyer Dispute in Joint Purchases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on payment and possession in joint purchase when one partner is absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the present buyer's right to force delivery and retention, and distinguish the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Necessity compels payment; a volunteer cannot recoup, but one compelled by the seller's retention right is no volunteer.

In joint purchases, if one buyer disappears, the present buyer may need to pay the entire price to take possession. Whether he can then force delivery of the absent partner's share—and withhold it—depends on the principle of necessity versus voluntariness. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the majority Hanafi view and the lone dissent of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (the two)
Identify what the majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold: the present buyer can pay all, take the whole item, and withhold the absent's share until reimbursement.
This is the relied-upon position.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing view
State that Abu Yusuf considers the present buyer a volunteer (mutabarri') and thus incapable of forcing delivery or seeking recourse.
Abu Yusuf alone dissents from the majority.
3
Follow each side's proof
Abu Yusuf: payment without authorization is voluntary, no compulsion. The two: the seller's retention right creates necessity—the buyer cannot access his own share without paying all.
Key dalil is the seller's right of retention (haqs al-habs).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The two rebut Abu Yusuf by arguing that the present buyer is compelled by the seller's retention right, not a volunteer. The necessity transforms the act from voluntary to obligatory.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under the two, the present buyer takes full possession and can withhold; under Abu Yusuf, he only takes his share and cannot force delivery of the absent's share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غاب إلخ: قيد بالغيبة؛ لأنه لو كان حاضراً لا يقبضه، ويكون متبرعاً بما أدى بالاتفاق، وقيد بـ"أحد المشتريين"؛ لأن أحد المستأجرين لو غاب قبل نقد الأجرة، فنقد الحاضر جميعاً كان متبرعاً، وقوله: "حتى ينقد" أي يؤدي، وهو في الأصل تميز جيد الدراهم من رديئها ثم استعمل في معنى الأداء، وهذا قول الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: = أحد المشتريين، فللحاضر دفع كل الثمن وقبضه وحبسه، حتى ينقد شريكه. ومن باع أمة بألف مثقال ذهب وفضة، فهما نصفان، وإن قضى زيفاً عن جيد، وهو لا يعلم، وتلف، فهو قضاء، = إذا نقد الحاضر كل الثمن لم يأخذ إلا نصيبه من المبيع بطريق المهايأة، وكان متبرعاً فيما أدى . عن والخلاف في مواضع: أحدها: في قبض جميع المبيع على تقدير إيفاء كل الثمن. والثاني: في حبس نصيب الغائب عنه إذا حضر. والثالث: في الرجوع عليه إذا أدى والرابع: في إجبار البائع على قبول ما أداه الحاضر من نصيب الغائب. والخامس: في إجبار البائع على تسليم نصيب الغائب إذا أوفاه الثمن كله، فعندهما: يجبر، وعند أبي يوسف: لا؛ إذ الحاضر قضى ديناً على الغائب بغير أمره فكان متبرعاً فيه، ولا يجبر على التبرع، ولا رجوع في المتبرعات، وهو أجنبي . نصيبه فلا يقبضه، ولهذا لو كان حاضراً يكون متبرعاً بالإجماع. ولهما: أن الحاضر مضطر إلى أداء كل الثمن؛ لأن للبائع حق حبس كل المبيع إلى أن يستوفي كل الثمن، فلا يكون متبرعاً بالاضطرار؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الانتفاع بملكه إلا بأداء الجميع، وهذا إذا كان الثمن حالاً، أما لو كان مؤجلاً فليس للحاضر دفعه وإن حل الأجل؛ لأن البائع لا يملك الحبس. (عيني، فتح) حبسه أي وله حبس المبيع عن شريكه إذا حضر . ( عيني)
And if one of them disappeared etc: it is restricted by absence because if he was present he would not take possession and would be a volunteer by what he paid by agreement. And restricted by 'one of the two buyers' because if one of two lessees disappeared before paying the rent, and the present paid all, he would be a volunteer. And his saying 'until he pays' means he performs, and originally it is distinguishing good dirhams from bad, then used in the meaning of payment. And this is the saying of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said: = One of the two buyers, so the present one may pay all the price and take possession and withhold until his partner pays. And whoever sells a slave-girl for a thousand mithqals of gold and silver, they are halves. And if he pays bad coins for good ones, and he does not know, and they are destroyed, then it is a settlement. = When the present one pays all the price, he takes only his share of the sold item by way of division, and he is a volunteer in what he paid. And the dispute is in places: one: in taking possession of all the sold item on the basis of paying all the price. Second: in withholding the absent partner's share from him when he returns. Third: in recourse against him if he paid. Fourth: in forcing the seller to accept what the present one paid of the absent partner's share. Fifth: in forcing the seller to deliver the absent partner's share if he pays the entire price. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is forced; according to Abu Yusuf, no, because the present one paid a debt on behalf of the absent without his order, so he is a volunteer, and one cannot be forced to volunteer, and there is no recourse in voluntary acts, and he is a stranger. So he does not take his share, and therefore if he were present, he would be a volunteer by consensus. And for them (the two): the present one is compelled to pay all the price because the seller has the right to withhold all the sold item until he receives all the price, so he is not a volunteer due to necessity because he cannot enjoy his property except by paying all. And this is when the price is due; if it is deferred, the present one is not to pay it even if the term expires because the seller has no right of retention.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scenario of two partners buying an item, one of whom disappears before paying his share. The present buyer pays the entire price and takes possession of the whole item. The question is whether the present buyer can force the seller to deliver the absent partner's share, and whether he can withhold it from the absent partner when he returns. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two), the present buyer can force the seller to deliver the absent's share, and he can also withhold it until the absent pays his share. Abu Yusuf alone disagrees, arguing that the present buyer paid voluntarily without authorization, so he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and cannot force the seller or withhold the absent's share. The two respond that the present buyer is compelled by necessity because the seller has a right to retain the entire item until full payment, so the payment is not voluntary. The dispute also extends to whether the present buyer can have recourse against the absent partner: the two say yes, Abu Yusuf says no. The text also notes that if the price is deferred (not immediately due), the present buyer cannot pay it even if the term expires because the seller cannot retain the item. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), as the majority of the Hanafi school follows them.

Key Terms
متبرع (mutabarri'): A volunteer who performs an act without legal compulsion or expectation of reimbursement.
حبس (habs): Right of retention; withholding possession until a claim is satisfied.
اضطرار (idhtirar): Compulsion or necessity that removes the legal character of voluntariness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is the present buyer not considered a volunteer when he pays the entire price?
A) Because he has a contractual obligation to pay.
B) Because the seller's right of retention forces him to pay all to access his own property.
C) Because the absent partner authorized him in advance.
D) Because he expects a reward from the absent partner.

Key Takeaway
In joint purchases, the present buyer who pays the full price due to the seller's retention right is not a volunteer; he may take the entire item and withhold the absent partner's share.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Interpreting Mixed Gold and Silver Prices in Sales
Default division of mixed gold/silver price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default division when a price is specified as 'X mithqals of gold and silver' and distinguish the rule when 'min' is used.

Core Idea
If a price is 'gold and silver', each is half; if 'from gold and silver', custom decides.

When a sale price is expressed as a mixed amount of gold and silver (e.g., 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver'), the Hanafi school defaults to equal halves unless the wording indicates otherwise. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and customary principles that determine the division, including the crucial distinction between the conjunctive 'and' (waw) and the preposition 'min' (from). Understanding this ruling prevents ambiguity in sales, loans, and other contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative phrase
Find the exact wording used in the price term: is it 'alf mithqal dhahab wa fidda' (with waw) or 'alf min al-dhahab wa al-fidda' (with min)? The ruling depends on this distinction.
The waw without min implies equal partnership.
2
Apply the default division for 'waw'
If the phrase uses 'wa' (and) without 'min', the rule is 'فهما نصفان' – they are halves. So a thousand mithqals means 500 gold and 500 silver.
This is because the word mithqal is added equally to both.
3
Recognize the exception with 'min'
If the phrase uses 'min' (from), as in 'alf min al-dhahab wa al-fidda', then custom determines the weight: gold is measured in mithqals, silver in dirhams (7 dirhams per mithqal).
The treasury and merchants use this standard.
4
Extend the principle to other goods
The same equal division applies to any measured, weighed, or counted item: e.g., 'a kurr of wheat, barley, and sesame' gives one third each.
Quality must also be specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهما نصفان فيجب من كل منهما خمس مائة مثقال. So they are halves, so from each he must pay five hundred mithqals. (T) Because he added the mithqal to both equally, so from each one half is due due to lack of priority, and it is required to specify the quality of goodness and others. And this division is established in everything that is measured, weighed, or counted, whether as a loan, salam, usurpation, deposit, dowry, bequest, or guarantee. And among it is if he said: I owe a kurr of wheat, barley, and sesame, then he owes a third of each. Unlike if he bought a slave-girl for a thousand from gold and silver, where from gold he owes mithqals, i.e., five hundred, and from silver, dirhams, because he added the thousand to them both, so it is directed to the customary weight of each, and the customary in gold is the mithqal, and in silver is the dirham of weight seven (Ayni, Fath) and it is what the treasury accepts and merchants take.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how to divide a price when it is specified as a mixed amount of gold and silver, e.g., 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver'. The ruling is that each component is half of the total, so 500 mithqals of gold and 500 mithqals of silver. This is because the phrase 'gold and silver' is interpreted as equal parts by default. However, if the phrase is 'a thousand from gold and silver' (using 'min'), then it means a thousand from the two, but the specific weights are determined by customary usage: gold is measured in mithqals, silver in dirhams (7 to a mithqal). The same principle of equal division applies to other things like grains (kurr of wheat, barley, sesame gives a third each) or other fungible goods. The passage also notes that quality (joodah) must be specified.

Key Terms
ألف مثقال ذهب وفضة (alf mithqal dhahab wa fidda): A thousand mithqals of gold and silver collectively, interpreted as 500 mithqals each.
مثقال (mithqal): A unit of weight for gold; approximately 4.25 grams.
درهم (dirham): A unit of weight for silver; in Hanafi custom, 10 dirhams = 7 mithqals (so 1 dirham = 0.7 mithqal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) 500 mithqals of gold and 250 dirhams of silver.
D) 250 mithqals of gold and 250 dirhams of silver.

Key Takeaway
When a price is expressed as 'X mithqals of gold and silver', the default is equal halves unless the wording 'min' shifts to customary weights.

Paying Bad Coins for Good: The Settlement Dispute
Dispute on settlement with bad coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on paying bad coins for good ones, distinguish the views of the two (Sahibayn) and Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi ruling with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
If bad coins are paid for good ones and the creditor consumes them unknowingly, the debt is settled according to the two (Sahibayn).

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the settlement of a debt when the debtor pays with defective currency. The key question: does the creditor's unknowing acceptance and consumption of bad coins discharge the debt completely, or does he retain a right to recover good coins? We will follow the reasoning of both sides and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the exact facts: a debtor owes good dirhams but pays with counterfeit (bad) dirhams. The creditor accepts them unknowingly and later consumes or loses them. Then he discovers the defect.
Note that the creditor's ignorance at the time of receipt is crucial.
2
State the position of the two
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the debt is fully settled. The creditor has no further claim against the debtor. This is the literal ruling of the text: 'فهو قضاء'.
3
State Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf (alone, contrary to his usual agreement with Muhammad in the Sahibayn) holds that the creditor may return an equivalent amount of bad coins and claim the good ones from the debtor.
This is a rare instance where Abu Yusuf differs from Muhammad.
4
Analyze the proof for each view
The two rely on the finality of consumption: since the creditor accepted and used the coins, the transaction is complete. Abu Yusuf argues that the creditor's right includes quality (description) as well as quantity; since the defect was unknown, he can restore the defective payment and demand proper quality.
Abu Yusuf's proof is based on the principle that the right is observed in both description and quantity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the two: the debt is settled. The text indicates this by its phrasing and the general acceptance of this view.
Thus, in practice, if a creditor unknowingly consumes bad coins, he cannot later demand good ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن جيد بأن كان له دراهم جياد على رجل فقضاه زيفاً: زيوفا بدلا عن جياد (عيني) وهو : أي الذي قبض زيوفا لا يعلم أنها زيوف. (محشي) وتلف: أي الزيوف عنده بأن هلك أو أنفق ثم علم بالعيب. (عيني) فهو قضاء صورته: رجل له على رجل آخر عشرة دراهم جياد، فقضاه زيوفا، والقابض لم يعلم فأنفقها أو هلكت ثم علم أنها كانت زيوفا فهو قضاء لحقه، فلا يكون له غيره عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف يرد مثل زيوفه، ويرجع بالجياد؛ لأن حقه يراعى من حيث الوصف من حيث القدر، إلا أنه تعذر عليه الرجوع بالجودة؛ لعدم انفكاكه، فيرد مثل المقبوض ويرجع بالجياد؛ ليكون - كما يراعي =

(Mahshi) And they perished: i.e., the bad coins perished with him, either destroyed or spent, then he became aware of the defect. (Ayni) So it is a settlement. Its picture: a man has ten good dirhams owed by another man, so he paid him bad ones, and the recipient did not know, then he spent them or they perished, then he knew they were bad. So it is a settlement of his right, and he has nothing else according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the payment of a debt with bad (counterfeit) coins when the creditor is unaware and the coins are later lost or spent. The scenario: a debtor owes ten good dirhams but pays with ten bad dirhams. The creditor, unaware, accepts and uses them, then later discovers they are bad. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the debt is considered fully settled; the creditor has no further claim. According to Abu Yusuf alone, the creditor may return an equivalent amount of bad coins (since the original bad coins are gone) and claim the good ones from the debtor. The reasoning of Abu Yusuf is that the creditor's right encompasses both quantity and quality; since the quality is lost through no fault of his own, he can restore the counterpart and demand the proper quality. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), i.e., the debt is settled.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Settlement; full discharge of a debt.
زيف (zayf): Counterfeit or bad coins; defective currency.
الطرفان (al-tarafan): The two; referring to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on paying bad coins for good, the relied-upon position is that the debt is fully settled if the creditor consumes the bad coins unknowingly.

Forcing the Seller to Deliver the Absent Partner's Share
Intra-Hanafi dispute on forcing seller to deliver absent partner's share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on forcing the seller to deliver the absent partner's share and evaluate the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Necessity transforms a volunteer into a compelled actor: seller's lien forces present buyer to pay full price.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a joint purchase where one buyer is present and the other absent. The central question is whether the seller can be forced to deliver the absent partner's share when the present buyer pays the full price. The dispute turns on whether the present buyer is a volunteer (mutabarri') or compelled by necessity (idtirar). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the concept of necessity in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common scenario
Two buyers jointly purchase an item; one is present, one is absent. The present buyer pays the full price to the seller to obtain his own share.
The seller has the right to withhold the entire item until full payment.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
The seller is forced to deliver the absent partner's share because the present buyer is compelled by necessity – he cannot access his property without paying the full price.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
The seller is not forced because the present buyer is a volunteer – he paid the absent's debt without authorization, and volunteers cannot be forced.
4
Analyze the proof of each side
The two argue from the seller's lien: the seller's right to detain the entire item compels the present buyer. Abu Yusuf argues from general principles of debt: paying another's debt without command is voluntary and unenforceable.
The two use rational proof (dalil aqli) based on necessity.
5
Determine the practical consequence and mufta bihi
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of the two: the seller must deliver. This ensures the present buyer can obtain his property without delay.
This applies only when the price is immediately due (halan), not deferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد المشتريين، فللحاضر دفع كل الثمن وقبضه وحبسه، حتى ينقد شريكه. ومن باع أمة بألف مثقال ذهب وفضة، فهما نصفان، وإن قضى زيفاً عن جيد، وهو لا يعلم، وتلف، فهو قضاء، = ولم يعين أي الذهب والفضة صاحبه، : إذا نقد الحاضر كل الثمن لم يأخذ إلا نصيبه من المبيع بطريق المهايأة، وكان متبرعاً فيما أدى . عن والخلاف في مواضع: أحدها: في قبض جميع المبيع على تقدير إيفاء كل الثمن. والثاني: في حبس نصيب الغائب عنه إذا حضر. والثالث: في الرجوع عليه إذا أدى والرابع: في إجبار البائع على قبول ما أداه الحاضر من نصيب الغائب. والخامس: في إجبار البائع على تسليم نصيب الغائب إذا أوفاه الثمن كله، فعندهما: يجبر، وعند أبي يوسف: لا؛ إذ الحاضر قضى ديناً على الغائب بغير أمره فكان متبرعاً فيه، ولا يجبر على التبرع، ولا رجوع في المتبرعات، وهو أجنبي . نصيبه فلا يقبضه، ولهذا لو كان حاضراً يكون متبرعاً بالإجماع. ولهما: أن الحاضر مضطر إلى أداء كل الثمن؛ لأن للبائع حق حبس كل المبيع إلى أن يستوفي كل الثمن، فلا يكون متبرعاً بالاضطرار؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الانتفاع بملكه إلا بأداء الجميع، وهذا إذا كان الثمن حالاً، أما لو كان مؤجلاً فليس للحاضر دفعه وإن حل الأجل؛ لأن البائع لا يملك الحبس.
One of the two buyers: the present [buyer] may pay the full price, take possession, and detain [the item] until his partner pays. And whoever sells an enslaved woman for one thousand mithqals of gold and silver, they are half and half. And if he pays debased [coins] for good ones, and he does not know, and they are lost, it is settlement. And if he did not specify which gold or silver: if the present buyer pays the full price, he takes only his share of the sold item by way of division, and he is a volunteer in what he paid. And the disagreement is in issues: first, taking possession of the entire sold item on the basis of paying the full price; second, detaining the absent partner's share from him when he appears; third, recourse against him when he pays; fourth, forcing the seller to accept what the present buyer paid for the absent's share; fifth, forcing the seller to deliver the absent's share if he paid the full price. According to the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad], he is forced; according to Abu Yusuf, no: because the present buyer paid a debt for the absent without his command, so he is a volunteer in it, and one is not forced to volunteer, and there is no recourse in voluntary acts, and he is a stranger. His share he does not take, and thus if he were present, he would be a volunteer by consensus. And for the two: the present buyer is compelled to pay the full price because the seller has the right to detain the entire sold item until he receives the full price, so he is not a volunteer due to compulsion; because he cannot benefit from his property except by paying everything. And this is when the price is immediately due; but if it were deferred, the present buyer may not pay it even if the term has come due, because the seller does not have the right to detain.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding a sale with two buyers where one is present and the other absent. The matn states that the present buyer may pay the entire price and take possession of the entire sold item, detaining it until the absent partner pays his share. However, the commentary from 'Ayni and Fath al-Qadir clarifies that the present buyer only takes his own share through a distribution (muhaya'ah) and is considered a volunteer (mutabarri') in the excess payment. The dispute centers on five issues, the fifth being whether the seller can be forced to deliver the absent partner's share when the present buyer pays the full price. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), the seller is compelled because the present buyer is acting under duress: the seller's right of lien (right to withhold the goods until full payment) forces the present buyer to pay the entire price to access his own share. Abu Yusuf argues that the present buyer is a volunteer because he paid another's debt without authorization, and one cannot be forced to accept a voluntary payment. The two's view is based on necessity (idtirar): the present buyer cannot benefit from his property without paying the full price. This reasoning applies only when the price is immediately due (halan); if the price is deferred, the present buyer has no right to pay early because the seller has no lien on a deferred price. The commentary emphasizes the distinction between voluntary and compelled payments.

Key Terms
المهايأة (al-muhaya'ah): Division or apportionment of the sold item between the partners proportionally.
متبرع (mutabarri'): A volunteer who acts without compensation or without being obligated.
اضطرار (idtirar): Compulsion or necessity that removes the characterization of being a volunteer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why is the present buyer not considered a volunteer?
A) Because the price is deferred
B) Because the absent partner authorized him
C) Because he is compelled by the seller's right of lien
D) Because he intends to resell at a profit

Key Takeaway
The decisive view (mufta bihi) is that necessity (idtirar) overrides the characterization of volunteerism, compelling the seller to deliver the absent partner's share when the present buyer pays the full price.

Ownership of Game on Another's Land
Ruling on acquisition of game animals on another's land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who owns wild animals or their offspring found on another's land, and explain the key distinction based on whether the land is prepared for hunting.

Core Idea
A wild animal on your land is not yours unless you prepared for it or it's a natural increase.

If a bird lays eggs in your garden, do you own the eggs? Instinct may say yes, but Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between mere presence and intentional acquisition. This lesson unpacks the ruling that ownership follows the first taker unless the landowner has prepared the land for hunting, or the item is a natural increase of the land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The text states that if a bird hatches, lays eggs, or a deer takes shelter in a man's land, the offspring or animal belongs to the person who takes it – not the landowner. Note that this applies to wild, ownerless (mubah) animals.
The ruling covers eggs, chicks, and the deer itself.
2
Recognize the general principle: mubah property
The animals are mubah (ownerless). Hanafi fiqh holds that mubah property becomes owned by the first person to take possession (ihraz). The landowner has no special claim merely because the animal is on his land.
This is the baseline ruling.
3
Understand the exception for prepared land
If the land is prepared (mu'adda) for hunting – e.g., the landowner has set traps or designated the area for trapping – then he does own the catch. The text analogizes to a net set for drying that accidentally catches game: no ownership without intent. But if the net is set for hunting, ownership follows.
Intent and preparation change the ruling.
4
Examine the honeybee exception
Honeybees and their honeycomb on one's land are owned by the landowner even if the land is not prepared for them. This is istihsan – because honey is considered a natural increase of the land (ziyada), like trees or soil deposited by water.
This is an exception to the general rule, based on a different legal reasoning.

(عيني، فتح)
And if a bird hatch or lay eggs, or a deer enter its cover, in a man's land, then it is for the one who takes it. (Ayni) 'Aw takannas': i.e. he hid; its meaning originally: he entered the kanas, which is the place of the deer; and in a copy 'takassar' i.e. his legs, and if he broke one, it would be for him. So it is for the one who takes it [i.e. the mentioned chick, egg, and deer, not for the owner of the land; because it is mubah (free) whose hand has preceded to it. (Ayni)] This is if his land is not prepared for that; but if it is prepared for hunting, then it is for him; because the ruling is not attached to a valid cause except by contract. Do you not see that one who sets up a net for drying, and game gets caught in it, or digs a well for water, and game falls into it, does not own it, and he is not liable for penalty if he is in ihram; but if he intended by it hunting, he owns it and the penalty is obligatory upon him. And likewise the detail if game enters his house, or what is scattered of dirhams falls into his clothes, then he does not own it unless he collects it or is prepared for it. Unlike bees' honeycomb on his land, where he owns it even if his land is not prepared for it; because it is from the increases of the land, so he owns it as a follow-up, like trees that grow in it or soil that accumulates in it by the flow of water, even if his land is not prepared for that. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its reasoning (ta'lil) concerning the ownership of animals and their offspring found on another's land. The core ruling: if a bird hatches, lays eggs, or a deer takes shelter in someone's land, the offspring or the animal itself belongs to the person who takes (catches) it, not to the landowner. The reason given is that these are mubah (free) items; the first hand to take possession acquires ownership. The commentary from Ayni emphasizes that the landowner does not own them merely by virtue of his land. However, an exception is made if the land is prepared (mu'adda) for hunting or trapping. In that case, the landowner owns it because he intended that the land serve as a means of acquisition, analogous to setting a net or digging a well with the intention of trapping. The Hanafi position further distinguishes the case of honeybees: honeycombs on one's land are owned by the landowner even if the land is not prepared, because they are considered a natural increase of the land (ziyada), like trees that grow or soil deposited by water. The passage also draws analogies: if game enters a house or scattered money falls into one's clothes, ownership does not transfer unless the person intentionally collects or prepares for it. This ruling is based on the principle that mere occurrence on one's property does not confer ownership unless there is intention and preparation. The authority weight: this is from the commentaries of Ayni and Fath (from the Hanafi tradition), relying on the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
مُبَاح (mubah): Free or ownerless property that can be acquired by taking possession.
أَخْذ (akhdh): Taking physical possession with the intention of ownership.
مُهَيَّأ (mu'adda): Prepared or intended for a specific purpose, here for hunting or trapping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a bird lays eggs in a man's land that is not prepared for hunting, who owns the eggs?
A) The landowner because it is in his land
B) The person who takes the eggs
C) The state (government)
D) No one; eggs remain unowned

Key Takeaway
Ownership of mubah property on another's land depends on intention and preparation; mere location is insufficient.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
15 min
Seven Transactions Nullified by a Corrupt Condition (Shart Fasad)
Enumeration of transactions voided by a corrupt condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that contracts exchanging property for property are voided by corrupt conditions, identify the seven enumerated transactions subject to this ruling, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on revocation (raj'a).

Core Idea
A corrupt condition turns an exchange contract into riba by adding benefit without countervalue.

Imagine you buy a car and the seller says, 'I'll sell it to you on condition that you lend me $1,000.' Is the sale valid? According to the Hanafi school, such a condition corrupts the sale because it adds an extra benefit without consideration. This lesson unpacks the principle behind this ruling and enumerates seven types of transactions that become void (fasid) when a corrupt condition is attached. We will also explore the key distinction between suspension (ta'liq) and stipulation (tashrit), and address a scholarly disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding revocation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Understand that every contract that is an exchange of property for property (mubadala mal bi mal) is nullified by a corrupt condition. Voluntary gifts and non-financial exchanges (like marriage) are not voided; only the condition fails.
The hadith 'He forbade a sale and a condition' (نهى عن بيع وشرط) is the proof.
2
Distinguish Ta'liq from Tashrit
Suspension with 'in' (if) is generally permissible in sales except when it creates an option for a third party without a time limit. Stipulation with 'ala' (on condition) is evaluated: if the condition is natural to the contract (e.g., delivery, payment term), it is valid; if it adds unilateral benefit, the contract becomes corrupt.
This distinction is crucial for determining when a condition voids the contract.
3
Recognize the Seven Transactions
The passage enumerates: 1) sale (bay'), 2) division (qisma), 3) hire (ijara), 4) ratification (ijaza), 5) revocation (raj'a), 6) settlement (sulh) for property of different genus, and 7) release (ibra') from debt. Each is voided by a corrupt condition because they involve mubadala mal bi mal or similar exchange of benefits.
Note the specific examples given for each, such as selling a slave with service condition.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Raj'a
The author of al-Bahr objects that revocation (raj'a) is valid under jest like marriage, so it should not be voided by a condition. The author of al-Nahr responds that revocation lacks witnesses and financial consideration, so it differs from marriage and is corrupted by a condition.
This dispute shows the nuance within the Hanafi school on what constitutes exchange of property.
5
Apply the Ruling to Modern Scenarios
For example, if a landlord rents a property on condition that the tenant gives a loan, the hire contract becomes corrupt (fasid). Similarly, selling a car with a condition that the buyer provides a free service voids the sale.
Always check whether the condition is natural to the contract or adds extra benefit.

ما يبطل بالشرط الفاسد والأصل فيه أن كل ما كان مبادلة مال بمال يبطل بالشروط الفاسد؛ لما عليه 'نهى عن بيع وشرط'، وما كان مبادلة مال بغير مال، أو كان من التبرعات لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة؛ لأن الشروط الفاسدة من باب الربا؛ لأن الربا هو الفضل الخالي عن العوض، وحقيقة الشروط الفاسدة زيادة ما لا يقتضيه العقد، فيكون فيه فضل خال عن العوض وهو الربا ولا يتصور ذلك في المعاوضات غير المالية کالنكاح والطلاق على مال ونحو ذلك، ولا في التبرعات فيبطل الشرط [تبيين الحقائق: ٥٤٢/٤] وأصل آخر: وهو أن التعليق بالشرط المحض لا يجوز في التمليكات. وإذا عرف هذا، فنقول : البيع مبادلة مال بمال، فيفسد بالشروط الفاسدة لما روينا، ويجوز تعليقه بالشرط مطلقاً إن كان الشرط بكلمة 'إن'، سواء كان الشرط نافعاً أو ضاراً إلا في صورة واحدة، وهو أن يقول: بعت منك هذا إن رضي فلان، فإنه يجوز إذا وقته بثلاثة أيام؛ لأنه اشتراط الخيار للأجنبي، وهو جائز، وإن كان الشرط بكلمة 'على'، فإن كان الشرط مما يقتضيه العقد، أو يلائمه، أو فيه أثر ، أو جرى التعامل به كتسليم المبيع أو الثمن أو التأجيل أو الخيار، لا يفسد، ويجوز الشرط، وإن كان لا يقتضيه العقد ولا يلائمه ولا العادة جرت به، فإن كان في الشرط منفعة لأهل الاستحقاق فسد البيع، وإلا فلا.
